And Ibn Jarir and Ibn Abi Hatim narrated from the Mother of the Believers Aisha al-Siddiqa: 'Their firmness in knowledge was that they believed in its muhkam and mutashabih and did not know its interpretation.' And Abd ibn Hamid and Ibn Jarir narrated from Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz: ended. The knowledge of the firmly grounded in knowledge of the interpretation of the Quran until they said: 'We believe in it; all is from our Lord' (Al-Imran 3:7). And similar from Abu al-Sha'tha' and Abu Nahik. All the reports are in al-Durar al-Manthur. For us is the stopping at His saying: 'And none knows its interpretation except Allah' (Al-Imran 3:7). So it is not possible to coordinate His saying 'and those firmly grounded in knowledge' with it, because stopping at the coordinated element before mentioning the coordinator in a place of ambiguity is impossible, as all the reciters agree. Rather the memorizers of the time say it is a necessary stop. And whoever objects by saying that stopping on part of a sentence is valid, as it is authentically reported that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) stopped at 'Al-hamdu lillahi rabbi al-alameen' (Fatiha 1:2), has been heedless and confused the place of impossibility with the place of permissibility. And for us is His saying 'and those firmly grounded in knowledge' as a new beginning (istinaf), so it does not connect with what precedes. The reading of Ibn Mas'ud: 'Its interpretation is only with Allah, and those firmly grounded in knowledge say: We believe in it' narrated by Ibn Abi Dawud from al-A'mash from him, may Allah be pleased with him, likewise in al-Durar al-Manthur. And the istinaf here is determined because the word 'Allah' is in the genitive and 'al-rasikhun' is nominative. And the reading of Ubayy and Ibn Abbas: 'And those firmly grounded in knowledge say: We believe in it' narrated by Abd al-Razzaq and al-Hakim, and he authenticated it from Tawus from him, may Allah be pleased with him, likewise in al-Durar al-Manthur also. And it was declared weak in al-Taysir: the narration of Ubayy, may Allah be pleased with him. In this narration, the istinaf is also determined. And the irregular reading is hujja as previously mentioned, and at least it is one of the carriers of the mutawatir. Here I have something to say: The issue is not among those related to practical matters, but rather it is from the creedal matters, so a proof that provides certainty is required, and the irregular reading does not provide it, unless it is said: The intent is the prohibition of resolving upon interpretation for practical reasons and the context of the noble verse. And it is His saying: 'He it is who has sent down to you the Book; in it are verses decisive [muhkam] - they are the foundation of the Book - and others are ambiguous [mutashabih]. As for those in whose hearts is deviation, they follow that which is ambiguous thereof, seeking discord and seeking its interpretation. And none knows its interpretation except Allah. And those firmly grounded in knowledge say: We believe in it; all is from our Lord. And none remember except those of understanding.' (Al-Imran 3:7). For the deviators, whose share is seeking the interpretation of the ambiguous, must have an opposite. For Allah does not mention the ruling of a group except that He pairs the ruling of their counterpart. And the firmly grounded are worthy of being counterparts, so their share must be not interpreting, rather only believing in it. So 'those firmly grounded in knowledge' etc. is an istinaf to explain their share. Commentary
This passage is a dispute over whether the mutashabihat (ambiguous verses) of the Quran are understood by anyone. The author argues for the position of the Salaf (including the Hanafi school) that the knowledge of their interpretation is exclusive to Allah, and that the phrase 'and those firmly grounded in knowledge' in Quran 3:7 is not coordinated with 'except Allah' but is a new sentence (istinaf) indicating that they say 'We believe in it' without claiming to know the interpretation. The passage uses linguistic arguments (stopping rules in recitation) and cites various readings to support this. It also addresses the Shafi'i claim that all Quran is understood by some, and responds that even if irregular readings are used as proof, creedal matters require certainty, but the practical ruling of prohibiting seeking interpretation is supported by context. The author concludes that the firmly grounded do not seek interpretation but affirm faith.

Key Terms
المتشابه (al-mutashabih): Verses of the Quran whose meaning is unknown and not understood by anyone, only Allah knows them.
الوقف (al-waqf): The rule of pausing in recitation; here it determines the grammatical structure of the verse.
الاستئناف (al-istinaf): A new beginning or independent clause that does not depend grammatically on what precedes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author use the rule of pausing (waqf) in Quran 3:7 to support the claim that only Allah knows the interpretation of mutashabihat?
A) He says the waqf after 'illa Allah' is prohibited
B) He claims there is no waqf in the verse
C) He argues the waqf is necessary, making 'wa al-rasikhun' a new sentence
D) He says the waqf shows the verse is abrogated

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on mutashabihat relies on the necessary pause in Quran 3:7, making 'those firmly grounded' a new sentence affirming faith, not claiming knowledge of interpretation.

Do the Rashikhun Know the Ta'wil of Mutashabihat?
Debate on whether the deeply learned know the interpretation of ambiguous verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position on whether the rāsikhūn know the ta'wil of mutashabihat and distinguish between knowledge by acquisition and unveiling.

Core Idea
The rāsikhūn do not know the ta'wil by acquired knowledge; only Allah knows it with certainty.

This passage examines a key usul dispute over Quranic interpretation. Some argue that ambiguous verses must be fully knowable, but the Hanafi school holds that the deeply learned do not have certain knowledge of their interpretation by their own reasoning. Instead, any knowledge they have is a divine gift. The passage presents two objections and Hanafi responses, culminating in a reconciliation that the dispute is about acquired knowledge, not unveiling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The central dispute: whether the rāsikhūn (deeply learned) know the interpretation (ta'wil) of the ambiguous verses (mutashabihat).
This is rooted in the verse Al-Imran 3:7.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the rāsikhūn do not know the ta'wil by acquired knowledge; certain knowledge belongs only to Allah.
The verse is read with 'wa' as isti'naf, making the exception exclusive.
3
Analyze the opposing arguments
The source presents two objections: (1) if some verses were not understandable, addressing with unintelligible speech is far from Allah; (2) the Companions transmitted interpretations, proving knowledge is possible.
The second objection relies on reported tafsir.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
First response: The Prophet (peace be upon him) understood everything; the rest are not addressees in that sense; ambiguous verses test faith. Second response: Companion interpretations were conjectural (takhmin), not certain.
This undermines the claim of certain knowledge.
5
Reconcile the positions
The author concludes both sides agree on the possibility of knowledge by divine unveiling (kashf); the dispute is only about knowledge by acquisition (kasb).
Thus, the Hanafi position does not deny all knowledge, only certain acquired knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: لو لم يكن بعضه مفهوماً لزم الخطاب بما لا يفهم والخطاب بما لا يفهم بعيد منه تعالى. قلنا: أولاً: لعل المخاطب به رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام، وهو فاهم والنزاع إنما هو فيمن سواه عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام. وثانياً: ذلك البعد إذا لم يكن الغرض الابتلاء بإيجاب اعتقاد الحقية مجملاً وكبح عنان الذهن عن طلب التأويل. وقالوا ثانياً: نقل التأويل عن الصحابة والتابعين للمتشابهات وغيرها، فيكون إجماعاً على صحة حصول العلم. قلنا: لو سلم صحة النقل فلا نسلم أنهم أولوا يقيناً. والكلام في العلم حقيقة كما في المحكمات، وإنما تكلموا تخميناً لا على أنه تأويل عندهم. فافهم. وأعلم أن دلائل الفريقين منطبقة على العلم بالكسب وعدمه كما في المحكمات، فلا يبعد يكون النزاع فيه لا في العلم الكشفي الذي ينال من غير اختيار من العبد فافهم.
They said: If some part of the Qur'an was not understandable, addressing with what is not understood would be necessary, and addressing with what is not understood is far from Him. We said: First, perhaps the addressee is the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings upon him and his Companions), and he understands, and the dispute is only about others besides him. Second, that distance is when the purpose is not to test by obligating belief in the truth in a general way and to restrain the mind from seeking interpretation. And they said, secondly: The transmission of interpretation from the Companions and Followers for the ambiguous and other verses is [reported], so it is consensus on the validity of obtaining knowledge. We said: Even if the transmission is sound, we do not concede that they interpreted with certainty. The discussion is about actual knowledge as in the clear verses, and they only spoke by conjecture, not as their interpretation. So understand. And know that the proofs of the two groups are consistent regarding knowledge by acquisition or its absence as in the clear verses, so the dispute is not far from being about that, not about the unveiling knowledge reached without the servant's choice. So understand.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi usul dispute regarding the interpretation of mutashabihat (ambiguous verses). The author first attributes an objection to those who claim that the entire Qur'an is knowable: if some verses were not understandable, it would necessitate an address that is not understood, which is beneath Allah. The Hanafi response (the author's position) is twofold: (1) The primary addressee was the Prophet (peace be upon him), who understood everything; the argument is about others. (2) The purpose of ambiguous verses is to test belief in the general truth of the revelation and to prevent overreaching with interpretation. Then a second objection is raised: that interpretations from the Companions prove that knowledge is possible. The response is that those interpretations were only conjectural, not certain (yaqini). The author concludes by reconciling that both groups agree on the possibility of knowledge by acquisition vs. unveiling (kashfi). The relied-upon position is that the rāsikhūn do not have certain knowledge of ta'wil by their own effort; such knowledge, if attained, is a divine gift. The passage uses terminology from the Quranic verse (Āl 'Imrān 3:7) and discusses the meaning of 'istithnā' (exception) and the state (ḥāl) of 'yaqūlūn'.

Key Terms
المتشابهات (al-mutashābihāt): Verses of the Quran whose apparent meaning is not clear and require interpretation, often similar in one aspect but different in another.
الراسخون في العلم (al-rāsikhūn fī al-ʿilm): Those deeply rooted in knowledge, specifically in understanding the Quran and its sciences.
التأويل (al-ta'wīl): Interpretation of a verse to a meaning different from its apparent meaning based on evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, do the rāsikhūn (deeply learned) know the interpretation (ta'wil) of mutashābihat?
A) Yes, they know it with certainty like the clear verses.
B) No, they do not know it at all, not even by divine gift.
C) They may know it by divine unveiling (kashf), but not by acquired knowledge.
D) They only know it through consensus (ijma).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the rāsikhūn do not have certain knowledge of mutashabihat's ta'wil by acquisition; their knowledge, if any, is a divine unveiling.

Critiquing the Divine Promise Argument for Value Substitution in Zakat
Critique and refinement of a Hanafi argument for value substitution in zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument from the divine promise of provision for value substitution in zakat and identify the usul principle that custom (urf) can modify an express textual ruling.

Core Idea
The verse's implicative indication cannot override the hadith's express command; custom, not implication, permits substitution.

This passage delves into a sophisticated Hanafi argument for allowing cash payment in zakat instead of specific commodities. The argument uses the divine promise of provision to justify substitution, but the author scrutinizes it thoroughly, revealing important usul principles about the hierarchy of textual indications and the role of custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Argument
State the Hanafi argument: Allah promised provision for all, gave wealth to the rich, obligated zakat for the poor, thus fulfilling the promise. Since a single commodity cannot cover diverse needs, substitution is necessary.
The argument relies on the verse of rizq (e.g., Quran 11:6) and the analogy of a bank check (hawalah).
2
Analyze the Three Objections
List the three initial objections: (1) Zakat is not the only fulfillment; (2) Zakat comes from multiple types covering all needs; (3) The specific commodity can be traded for other needs.
These objections critique the necessity of substitution.
3
Trace the Responses
Explain how the author repels each objection: (1) The promise is fulfilled through various means; (2) Zakat is not explicitly obligated in all forms, so substitution for clothing etc. is needed; (3) The hawalah analogy shows the purpose is the amount, not the form.
The author concedes the objections are repelled but raises a deeper issue.
4
Identify the Fourth Usul Problem
Recognize that the verse's indication is implicative (dalalah iltizamiyya) while the hadith's is express (ibarah). Implicative cannot override express.
This is a key usul principle: express indication (ibarah) is stronger than implicative (iltizam).
5
Understand the Resolution via Custom
The author resolves that custom (urf), not the implicative indication, is the changer. The verse provides a contextual indicator (qarina) that substitution is customary, thus no direct conflict arises.
Custom modifies the application of the express text without overriding its literal meaning.

المقصود من إيجاب الزكاة إيجاب هذا القدر وتخصيص الشاة والحبوب والثمار والحجرين بالذكر إنما هو لكونها أهون على أصحابها، وحينئذٍ اندفع الثالث وهذا غاية ما يقال في هذا المقام وتأمل فيه. ثم ههنا إشكال رابع هو أن دلالة آية الرزق على كون الإيجاب إنجازاً ، ثم على جواز الاستبدال بعلاقة اللزوم بعد تسليهما من قبيل الالتزامية البتة ودلالة الحديث على وجوب الشاة عبارة والإشارة لا تصلح مغيرة للعبارة، كيف وفيه صرف الأقوى لأجل الأضعف، وذلك مما لا يجوز أصلاً، والغاية في الجواب أن الصرف لأجل أن هذا قرينة على جواز الاستبدال عرفاً فالمغير هو العرف، لا لأجل دفع التعارض بين الإشارة والعبارة حتى يقال : العبارة متقدمة على الإشارة عند التعارض كما مر وفرق بين التغييرين يظهر بالتأمل الصادق هذا غاية الكلام وتأمل فيه، ثم بعد في الكلام كلام لأن التقرير المذكور في المتن وهذا يفيدان أن المقصود من الحديث إيجاب مالية الشاة فلا معنى للتعليل بدفع الحاجة لأن استنباط العلة عندنا ليس إلا للقياس ولا يجوز التعليل بالعلة القاصرة، وحينئذ لا يتجه الجواب بأن فائدة التعليل غير منحصرة في
(Reflect) and memorize, for it is clear and very apparent truth. And our noble scholars mentioned another aspect: that Allah Almighty promised to deliver the provisions of all servants, then He gave wealth to the rich and did not fulfill it for the poor. Then He obligated in their wealth a right for Himself, then He delegated their provision to them from His wealth. Thus He fulfilled the promise. Provisions are of various types: food, drink, clothing, etc., and a single item of wealth does not suffice for them. So it is known that substitution is permissible. So the change is not by causal reasoning, but by this indication, and causal reasoning accompanies it. Thus they said. But it is subject to scrutiny: Firstly, we do not accept that the obligation of zakat is the fulfillment of the promise of delivering provision. Rather, the promise is fulfilled in other ways, such as guidance to trade, agriculture, etc., and by inspiring the rich to give the poor what suffices them, and more. Secondly, even if we accept that the obligation is fulfillment, the obligation of zakat is from many types of wealth (animals, gold, silver, grains, fruits) and all are sufficient for types of provision, so it does not permit substitution nor indicate that the form is not considered in the Shari'ah's view. Thirdly, even if we accept that, it is possible that the wajib is the sheep in form, and it is sufficient for types of provision because one can trade it for food, clothing, etc. Thus, this obligation does not give permission to the zakat payer to substitute, but to the poor, and there is no dispute. Ultimately, it is said: Allah promised to deliver provision and He fulfills His promise; provision from any source is from Him and fulfills the promise. When He obligated for the poor from the payer's wealth and delivered it to him, it is known that this is part of the promise's fulfillment, not that fulfillment is confined to this. So the first objection is repelled. The form is insufficient for the promised provision because clothing is part of it, and since it was not explicitly obligated in any wealth, substitution with clothing etc. is necessitated. Thus the second objection is repelled. Then, the immediate understanding from a bank check (hawalah) on one of the drawer's treasurers to be spent on various needs is that those needs are fulfilled by that amount of money, whether by paying that in its specific form or by paying other money of equal amount. So it is known that the purpose of obligating zakat is to obligate this amount. The specification of sheep, grains, fruits, and the two precious metals (gold and silver) is only because they are easier for their owners. Thus the third objection is repelled. And this is the ultimate statement on this matter. Reflect. Then there is a fourth problem: that the indication of the verse of provision on the obligation being fulfillment, and then on the permissibility of substitution by necessary concomitance after both are conceded, is a merely implicative indication (dalalat iltizamiyya). And the indication of the hadith on the obligation of the sheep is express (ibarah), and implicative indication cannot change express indication. How can the stronger be set aside for the weaker? That is not permissible at all. The response is that the setting aside is because this (the verse) is a contextual indicator (qarina) on the permissibility of substitution by custom (urf). So the changer is custom, not to resolve conflict between implicative and express indications such that it is said: express takes precedence over implicative at the time of conflict, as previously mentioned. There is a difference between the two kinds of change, as appears with sincere reflection. This is the ultimate discussion. Reflect. Then, after this, there is discussion: the formulation (taqrir) mentioned in the matn and this indicate that the purpose of the hadith is to obligate the financial value of the sheep. So there is no sense in causal reasoning with repelling need, because extracting the cause (istinbat al-illa) with us is only for qiyas, and it is not permissible to reason with a deficient cause. Therefore, the response that the benefit of causal reasoning is not confined to...
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents and critically examines a Hanafi argument for substituting value in zakat based on the divine promise of provision. The argument states: Allah promised provisions for all; He gave wealth to the rich and obligated zakat for the poor, thus fulfilling the promise. Since different provisions (food, drink, clothing) require different resources, one type of wealth cannot cover all, so substitution is necessary. The author then critiques this argument with three objections: (1) The obligation of zakat is not the only fulfillment; Allah fulfills via trade, agriculture, etc. (2) Even if it is fulfillment, zakat is from multiple types that together cover needs. (3) The specific commodity can be traded for other needs. The author then responds to these objections, arguing that since zakat was not explicitly obligated in all forms, substitution for clothing etc. is permitted. The analogy of a financial draft (hawalah) is used: the purpose is to transfer a specific amount, not the specific form. The passage then raises a fourth usul problem: the verse's implicative indication (dalalat iltizamiyya) cannot override the express indication (ibarah) of the hadith mandating the sheep. The author resolves this by stating that the actual changer is custom (urf), not the implicative indication, so no conflict arises. The passage ends by noting that the matn's formulation shows the hadith aims at obligating the financial value, making causal reasoning unnecessary.

Key Terms
دلالة التزامية (dalālah iltizāmiyyah): Implicative indication: a meaning that is necessarily required by the literal meaning but not directly stated.
عبارة (ʿibārah): Express indication: the direct literal meaning of the text.
قرينة عرفية (qarīnah ʿurfiyyah): A contextual indicator based on customary practice that modifies the apparent meaning.
حوالة (hawālah): A transfer of debt or a bank check; used as an analogy to show that the amount, not the form, matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for allowing substitution of value in zakat when considering the divine promise of provision and the express hadith?
A) The verse of provision directly overrides the hadith.
B) Custom (urf) provides a contextual indicator that substitution is permissible, not the implicative indication alone.
C) The hadith is not authentic.
D) The Companions had a consensus on substitution.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) can modify the application of an express text when it provides a contextual indicator, but implicative indications alone cannot override express ones.

Dispute on Whether Kuffar Are Addressed with Furu'
Refuting an objection about hadith opposing Quran via kuffar not being addressed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position that kuffar are addressed with furu' and apply it to the validity of prior marriages after conversion.

Core Idea
A decisive Quranic text cannot be opposed by a solitary hadith, even if kuffar are argued to be unaddressed.

This passage addresses a critical usul dispute: are disbelievers addressed by the subsidiary rulings of fiqh? The objection that they are not threatens to overturn the ruling on prior marriages after conversion. The author defends the Hanafi position by demonstrating that the Quranic prohibition of combining more than four wives is decisive, and that even if kuffar were unaddressed, their prior marriages would be void, requiring renewal. We will walk through the arguments and counter-arguments to understand the thamarah al-khilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the dispute centers on whether kuffar are addressed by furu' (subsidiary rulings). The objector claims they are not, so prior marriages were all valid.
2
State the opposing view
Imam Fakhr al-Islam and others hold that kuffar are not addressed, so all prior marriages were valid, and after conversion the convert has a choice to keep any four.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response argues that the prohibition of combining more than four is agreed upon and decisive. Even if unaddressed, the marriages would be void ab initio, requiring renewal, not choice.
4
Present the author's conclusion
The author asserts that kuffar are addressed with penalties and transactions. Quran 4:3 permits up to four, and the excess is invalid by decisive text, which cannot be opposed by a solitary report.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a convert must immediately separate from wives beyond four without choice, versus having a choice under the opposing view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان تنزلاً، فإن قبل التأويل وإلا يترك العمل لمعارضة الكتاب القاطع، ولقائل أن يقول : الكفار ليسوا مخاطبين بالفروع عند الإمام فخر الإسلام وغيره فغيلان ونوفل لم يكونا قبل مخاطبين بالاقتصار على الأربع، فالأنكحة كلها كانت صحيحة وبعد الإسلام إذ قد توجه خطاب الاقتصار وعدم الجمع لا بد من مفارقة واحدة والتعين تحكم فلزم الخيار فلا معارضة لكتاب الله تعالى، ولك أن تقول : إن هذا غاية الكلام من قبل المشايخ التابعين للإمام محمد رحمه الله، لكن الأمر غير خفي عن الفطن فإنه قد تقدم أن توجه الخطاب بالنهي متفق عليه، ومع ذلك فبعد الإسلام قد توجه النهي عن الاجتماع وهو مسبب من كل من أنكحة كل فيفسد الكل لمقارنتها كل المفسد ، كما إذا نكح المسلم خمساً بعقد، ولا وجه للتخير، ثم إنهم إذا لم يكونوا مخاطبين بالخطابات الشرعية فلا صحة لأنكحتهم من قبل في نظر الشارع، فالزوجات كلهن أجنبيات في نظر الشارع، فيجب تجديد النكاح لا التخيير فالحق أنهم مخاطبون بالعقوبات والمعاملات ونكاح الأوائل منهن صحيح في نصر الشارع لكونها خالية من المفسد، ومأذوناً فيها لقوله: ﴿فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُمْ ﴾ [النساء : ٣] ونكاح الزائدة فاسد لوجود المفسد بالنص القاطع، فلا يقبل المعارضة بخبر الواحد هذا غاية الكلام فتأمل فيه.
It is a descent, for if it accepts interpretation [it is fine], otherwise the action is abandoned due to opposition to the decisive Quran. And it can be said: The disbelievers are not addressed with the subsidiary rulings according to Imam Fakhr al-Islam and others, so Ghaylan and Nawfal were not previously addressed to confine to four [wives]; thus all marriages were valid. After Islam, when the address of confinement and prohibition of combining [more than four] is directed, it is necessary to separate one, and specification is arbitrary, so choice is required, thus no opposition to the Book of Allah. And you may say: This is the utmost speech from the scholars following Imam Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, but the matter is not hidden from the intelligent, for it has preceded that the direction of prohibition is agreed upon. Despite that, after Islam the prohibition of combining is directed, which is caused by each of the marriages, so all are invalid due to their simultaneous [occurrence] with the invalidating factor, like when a Muslim marries five by one contract, and there is no room for choice. Then, if they were not addressed with legal addresses, their previous marriages would have no validity in the view of the Lawgiver, so all wives are strangers in the view of the Lawgiver, so renewal of marriage is obligatory, not choice. The truth is that they are addressed with penalties and transactions, and the marriage of the earlier ones among them is valid in the view of the Lawgiver because it is free from the invalidating factor, and it is permitted by His statement: 'Then marry what is pleasing to you' [Nisa 3], and the marriage of the additional one is invalid due to the existence of the invalidating factor by decisive text, so it cannot be opposed by a solitary report. This is the end of the speech; ponder it.

Commentary
This passage is a dispute over whether a hadith can oppose a Quranic verse. The author argues that the objection that kuffar are not addressed with furu' is invalid. He explains that even if they were not addressed before, after embracing Islam, they are addressed, so all marriages beyond four become invalid, and they must separate. He concludes that they are addressed with transactions, so the earlier marriages are valid up to four, and the rest are invalid based on the Quranic evidence.

Key Terms
مخاطب بالفروع (mukhaṭab bil-furūʿ): One who is addressed by the subsidiary legal rulings of fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) All marriages are invalid.

Key Takeaway
Kuffar are addressed with furu' related to transactions, so a convert's prior marriages exceeding four are automatically invalid, and separation is obligatory without choice.

Refuting the Claim of Ambiguity in 'Wamsahu bi Ru'usikum'
Proving non-ambiguity of 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum' via usul arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a verb with the preposition 'ba' does not produce ambiguity (ijmal) when the meaning can be determined by usul categories like 'urf and the default indication of the verb.

Core Idea
A Quranic command is not mujmal if its meaning can be determined by established usul principles, even if scholars differ on the exact scope.

The verse 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum' is a key text in wudu. Some Hanafis claimed it is ambiguous regarding the extent of wiping. This passage refutes that claim using two main arguments: the Companions' practice and the linguistic principle of verbs with 'ba'. Understanding this refutation sharpens your ability to distinguish true ambiguity from legitimate interpretive differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim of Ambiguity
Note that the passage refutes the claim that the verse is mujmal (ambiguous) regarding how much of the head to wipe. Some Hanafis, including the author of al-Hidayah, held this view.
The target is specifically the phrase 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum' with the preposition 'ba'.
2
Examine the First Proof – Historical (Companions' Practice)
The author argues: if the verse were ambiguous, the Companions would have paused and asked the Prophet for clarification. Since no such pause is reported (despite the importance of wudu), the verse must be clear. Consider the counterargument: the obligation of wudu was already known, so no pause was needed.
This proof uses the principle that if ambiguity existed, it would have been transmitted due to general necessity.
3
Examine the Second Proof – Linguistic (Verb with 'Ba' and 'Urf)
The author states that if no custom ('urf) intervenes, a verb transitive with 'ba' indicates some part (tab'id) of the object. If custom intervenes, it indicates a specific part accordingly. In either case, the meaning is determined – not ambiguous.
The commentator notes that the conclusion holds regardless of whether 'ba' is for connection or adhesion.
4
Analyze the Cross-Madhhab Dispute
Imam Malik required wiping the entire head (no custom), while Imam al-Shafi'i required only a part (custom accepted). Both derive clear rulings from the same verse, showing that the verse itself is not ambiguous – the difference is over external factors (custom).
The passage notes that both positions lack definitive proof, but the verse remains mubayyan.
5
Conclude the Status of the Verse
The verse is not mujmal; it is mubayyan (clear). The apparent doubt is only about the existence of custom, not about the linguistic meaning. True ambiguity (ijmal) would require equal probability of multiple meanings with no preference – here, both possibilities are based on clear principles.
The author also suggests a simpler argument: 'ba' is literal for adhesion, so wiping any part is true, which is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: لا إجمال في نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَامْسَحُوا بِرُءُوسِكُمْ ﴾ [المائدة: ٦] أي في المسح المتعدي بالباء وليس في كل فعل نسب إلى المحل بالباء كما توهم (خلافاً لبعض الحنفية) ومنهم صاحب الهداية» لنا أوّلاً كما أقول لو كان القول المذكور (مجملاً) لوقع التوقف في الصحابة، ولو وقع لنقل التوقف، لأنه مما يتوفر الدواعي إليه) لأن كل أحد يحتاج إليه لأن أمر الوضوء أهم يبتلى به كل أحد وفيه نظر ظاهر، فإن حكم الوضوء كان يعرفه كل أحد قبل نزول هذه الآية، وإنما هي مقررة، لأن الوضوء فرض بمكة، والآية مدنية، فلو كانت مجملة لتعين المراد بالعلم السابق ولا يلزم التوقف، فضلاً أن ينقل، ولو قرر بأن أمثالها من لو كانت نعم : خصوص بعض الأفعال يقتضي لكن هذا خلاف متصورات الجماهير، ثم هو أيضاً لا يضر المقصود أصله فتأمل (وإن طرأ) عرف كذلك أفاد البعض) أي (مطلقاً) أي بعض كان فلا إجمال أيضاً، فإن قلت : إذا احتمل الشقان من وأوجبوا مسح الكل في الوضوء (و) ادعى الإمام الشافعي وعبد الجبار وأبو الحسين المعتزليان (ثبوته) في نحو مسحت يدي بالمنديل) فإنه يفيد مسح اليد ببعض المنديل عرفاً، فأوجبوا مسح بعض الرأس ولو شعرة، ولا يخفى ما في لفظ الأدعاء من الإشارة إلى أن لا دليل في كلام الطرفين (وأجيب) بعدم تسليم فهم المسح ببعض المنديل، بل بالمنديل مطلقاً، وأما انفهام البعض فمن خارج هو أن لا يمسح بالكل عادة، لكن لما كان
Issue: There is no ambiguity in something like His statement: 'And wipe with your heads' — i.e., in the wiping that is transitive with the preposition 'ba', not in every verb attributed to a locus with 'ba' as was imagined (contrary to some Hanafis, among them the author of al-Hidayah). Our first proof: I say: if that statement were ambiguous, then the Companions would have paused (in understanding), and if they had paused, it would have been transmitted, because the motives to transmit it are strong, since everyone needs it because the matter of ablution is important and everyone is tested with it. But this is apparently questionable, for the ruling of ablution was known to everyone before this verse was revealed; the verse only confirmed it, because ablution was made obligatory in Mecca, and the verse is Medinan. So if it were ambiguous, the intended meaning would be determined by prior knowledge, and no pause would be necessary, let alone be transmitted. And if it were argued that similar cases, if ambiguous, would cause a pause and be transmitted, and this is counted among such cases, the scope of discussion would widen due to the lack of motives in other than ablution. So reflect. And our second proof: If no custom (urf) intervenes that allows its application to some, it indicates all, because the principle for a verb attributed to a related object is that it conveys its relation to all of it; if it conveys wiping all, there is no ambiguity. I say: the implication is denied because of the 'ba'. That is, we do not concede that the absence of custom indicates all; it would follow only if the verb were transitive by itself (without preposition). But when it is with the letter 'ba', then (it indicates) any amount, and this does not harm the original purpose, for the verse is absolute, not ambiguous. Unless it is said that it indicates a specific unknown amount when 'wiping' is made transitive with 'ba', according to the opponent, then both comprehensive indication and absolute indication are denied. Moreover, this is only correct if 'ba' is for connection; but if it is for adhesion (ilṣāq), then it is true for any part of the head, so it does not indicate all, and maybe the indication to all is denied even when it is transitive by itself. For the relation of a verb to its object only requires that the verb relates to it, whether it encompasses it or not. Yes, some specific verbs require it, but this contradicts the conceptions of the majority. And this also does not harm the original purpose. So reflect. And if a custom intervenes that allows its application to some, it indicates some — i.e., absolutely any part — so there is no ambiguity either. If you say: 'If both possibilities are probable without preference, then ambiguity arises,' I say: 'These two possibilities are two schools of thought.' That is, the original setup of the construction is for all, so it is immediately understood, except if a custom intervenes. So one who claims it did not intervene understands its literal meaning; one who claims it did intervene understands according to custom. Hence no ambiguity. The only doubt is about the existence of custom, which is removed by induction. Ignorance of the reality does not cause ambiguity; rather it is removed by examining indicators. It would have been better and sufficient to argue that 'ba' is literal in adhesion, so it is applied to it, and the meaning is: adhesion of wiping is true for wiping all or some, which is absolute, and there is no ambiguity. No need for this elaboration. Then he said: Then Imam Malik, Qadi Abu Bakr, and Ibn Jani (the grammarian) claimed that the truth is in the two positions — he meant (that wiping) is with comprehensive wiping, and they denied custom and made wiping all necessary in ablution. And Imam al-Shafi'i, Abd al-Jabbar, and Abu l-Husayn (the two Mu'tazilites) claimed that custom exists in something like 'I wiped my hand with a towel' — for it indicates wiping the hand with part of the towel customarily, and they made wiping part of the head, even a single hair, obligatory. It is not hidden that the word 'claim' indicates that there is no evidence in the statements of either side. And it was answered by not conceding that 'wiping' is understood as (wiping the hand) with part of the towel; rather (it is understood) with the towel absolutely. As for understanding some part, that is from extraneous factors, namely that one does not usually wipe with the whole towel. But because...
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed usul ruling about the Quranic verse 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum' (5:6) — a central text in ablution. The Hanafi position is that this phrase is not ambiguous (mujmal); rather it clearly indicates that a part of the head must be wiped, and the extent is determined by the literal meaning of the verb with the preposition 'ba'. The author engages in a lengthy refutation of those who claim ambiguity, including some Hanafis like the author of al-Hidayah. He first argues from the practice of the Companions: if the verse were ambiguous, they would have paused and asked the Prophet, and that would have been widely reported. But it is not reported, so it is not ambiguous. This argument is then critically examined: the verse was revealed after the obligation of wudu was already known in Mecca, so the Companions understood it from prior knowledge. The second argument is based on usul: when a verb is attributed to a locus with the preposition 'ba', and no custom ('urf) intervenes, the original rule is that it indicates the whole (al-kull). However, the commentator (presumably the author of Fawatih al-Rahmut) interjects that this conclusion is not automatic because of the 'ba'. He distinguishes between 'ba' as a mere connector (sila) and 'ba' for adhesion (ilṣaq). If 'ba' is for adhesion, then it is true for any part, so it does not indicate the whole. Ultimately, the author argues that regardless of whether one takes the word to indicate all or some, there is no ambiguity because both views are based on clear evidence. The dispute between Imam Malik (who required wiping the whole head) and Imam al-Shafi'i (who required only a part) is presented as two valid interpretations, but the text notes that both claims lack definitive proof. The Hanafi position is that wiping a quarter of the head is obligatory (an established ruling not fully explained here, but the verse is not ambiguous; it allows for the obligation of a part).

Key Terms
الإجمال (al-ijmal): Ambiguity in a legal text where the meaning is unclear and requires external explanation; the passage argues this verse is not mujmal.
الباء (al-ba): The preposition 'ba' in 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum'; it is discussed as indicating either tab'id (partiality), sila (connection), or ilṣaq (adhesion).
العرف (al-urf): Custom that affects the interpretation of a phrase; here, whether custom exists that 'wiping with something' implies only a part.
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Pausing or refraining from acting until clarification; the absence of tawaqquf among Companions is used as evidence of clarity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is one argument given in the passage to prove that 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum' is not mujmal?
A) The Companions asked the Prophet for clarification, and that is reported.
B) The verse was revealed before the obligation of wudu was known, so people would have stopped.
C) If the verse were ambiguous, the Companions would have paused and asked, but no such pause is transmitted, indicating clarity.
D) The word 'ru'us' is definite, so it includes the entire head.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum' is not mujmal; it clearly indicates wiping part of the head, though the exact extent is determined by additional evidence.

Refuting the Claim of Ijmal in the Verse of Wiping
Refutation of ijmal in the verse of wiping

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the argument that the verse on wiping is mujmal and evaluate the Hanafi refutation using the tool-indicating function of the preposition 'ba'.

Core Idea
The preposition 'ba' indicates the tool (hand), not partiality; thus the verse is unambiguous.

This lesson unpacks the first part of the passage where an opponent argues that the verse 'وامسحوا برؤوسكم' is ambiguous (mujmal) because the 'ba' implies a partial wiping of an unknown amount. The Hanafi scholar refutes this with three objections, focusing on the nature of the preposition and the role of the hand as the tool.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opponent's Argument for Ijmal
The opponent claims that since the verb 'mash' is transitive with 'ba', and the hand (tool) is the instrument, only a part of the head is wiped. That part is unknown, hence ambiguity.
This relies on the idea that 'ba' indicates partiality.
2
Analyze the First Refutation: Tool Inclusion Does Not Entail Partiality
The author argues that not including the tool does not necessitate partiality; one can wipe the whole head by passing the hand. The 'ba' indicates the tool, not a part.
This shifts the focus from locus to tool.
3
Analyze the Second Refutation: Intention Requirement
Wiping within washing the face is invalid because the intention is for washing, not wiping. Thus non-performance in ghusl does not prove partiality.
The required intention (qasd) separates the acts.
4
Analyze the Third Refutation: Sequence Obligation
The opponent's argument about sequence (tartib) is irrelevant; the non-performance in ghusl is due to sequence, not absence of partiality.
The author criticizes the opponent's reasoning as insufficient.
5
Conclude: No Ambiguity
The three refutations together show that the verse is not mujmal. The obligation is to wipe any part of the head using the hand, and the customary size of the hand (roughly quarter) is the amount.
This sets the stage for further discussion on the exact amount.

(So there is no ambiguity and no absolute wiping that includes every part. Rather, the absolute is for the wiping that includes the hand, so it is not far). On this, the obligation is according to the hand, not the quarter except by estimation. Shaykh Ibn al-Humam refuted this in Fath al-Qadir by saying: Then it would follow that one cannot be satisfied with water flowing over the head without passing the hand, and the ruling is contrary to that. It is not far to say: Sufficiency by the indication of the text, for the purpose of passing the wet hand is to convey moisture to the head, and it has been conveyed here without passing. Then we have another statement: Here the verb is treated as necessary, and the object is not estimated in the structure of speech; rather, the tool is understood due to the absence of wiping without a tool. So consideration is only given to what is the tool by which the attachment of wiping to the head is performed. As for inclusion, it is an additional matter not required by the speech, so it does not necessitate inclusion of the hand. Yes, if the object were estimated in the structure and wiping extended to it by itself, it would indicate its inclusion according to what is well-known, and since it is not, it is not. Understand. And the fair view is that the statement of our scholars here is problematic that the likes of our minds cannot understand. The apparent view by looking at the evidence is the obligation of absolute wiping attached to the head, whether upon all or part, any part it may be. Understand.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the Quranic verse on wiping the head (mash) in wudu, specifically whether the verse implies a specific amount or is ambiguous (mujmal). The author begins by presenting an argument that the verse necessitates ambiguity because the verb 'mash' is transitive with the preposition 'ba' which indicates the tool (the hand), thus requiring wiping with the hand which covers only a part of the head, and that part is not specified. The author then refutes this reasoning with three objections: First, the lack of inclusion of the tool does not necessitate partiality; one can wipe the whole head with the hand. Second, the fact that wiping is not performed within washing the face is due to the intention requirement, not due to partiality. Third, the opponent's argument about sequence is irrelevant. The author then discusses attempts to clarify the ambiguity through hadith about wiping the forelock, but notes that these hadith were revealed in Medina while the verse was revealed in Mecca, so they cannot be clarificatory. Then the author presents Shams al-A'imma's position that the obligation is to wipe quarter of the head based on the size of the hand, but Ibn al-Humam refutes this by pointing out that water flowing without hand movement is sufficient. The author concludes that the strongest view is the obligation of absolute wiping, whether partial or complete, as the evidence does not specify a particular amount.

Key Terms
الإجمال (al-ijmal): Ambiguity in a text that requires external clarification
الآلة (al-alah): The tool (hand) by which wiping is performed
المحل (al-mahall): The locus (head) upon which the action is performed

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi argument against the claim that the verse 'وامسحوا برؤوسكم' is mujmal?
A) The verse is abrogated by hadith.
B) The preposition 'ba' indicates the tool (hand), not partiality, and the custom of the hand determines the amount.
C) The verse explicitly mentions 'all of the head'.
D) Consensus among companions clarifies it.

Key Takeaway
The preposition 'ba' in wiping verse indicates the tool; thus the verse is not ambiguous, and the obligation is to wipe according to the custom of the hand.

Why 'Yad' and 'Qat‘' Are Not Mujmal in the Theft Verse
Debating whether the words 'yad' and 'qat‘' in the theft verse are ambiguous or clear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi argument for why the words 'yad' and 'qat‘' in Qur'an 5:38 are not mujmal, and evaluate the counterarguments and responses.

Core Idea
A word is not mujmal simply because it has multiple possible meanings; the primary literal meaning, supported by immediate understanding, eliminates ambiguity.

In this passage, we explore a classic usūl al-fiqh dispute over whether the words 'hand' (yad) and 'cut' (qat‘) in the Qur’anic verse on theft are ambiguous (mujmal). The Hanafi school firmly holds that these words are clear (not mujmal), based on linguistic principles and immediate understanding. Let's walk through the reasoning and the objections raised.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
The Hanafi position is that there is no ijmāl in the words 'yad' and 'qat‘' individually, and therefore no ijmāl in the verse as a whole.
The contrary view is held by a small group (shiridhma qalīla) who claim ijmāl.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafis argue that 'yad' literally means the entire arm to the shoulder (since one can say 'some of the hand'), and 'qat‘' literally means severance. The other possible meanings (wrist, wounding) are metaphorical, based on immediate understanding (tabādur). Thus, there is no genuine ambiguity.
Tabādur is a key Hanafi tool: the meaning that first comes to mind when the word is uttered without context.
3
Examine the opponents' reasoning
The opponents claim both words are equivocal (mushtarak) between multiple literal meanings, and lacking a qarīna (contextual indicator), they become mujmal.
For them, ‘yad’ could equally mean arm to shoulder or hand to wrist; ‘qat‘’ could mean severance or wounding.
4
Follow the Hanafi responses to objections
The text presents three objections and rebuttals: (1) Using preponderance to negate ijmāl is establishing language by opinion (forbidden) → rebutted because negating ijmāl is a necessary implication, not a linguistic ruling. (2) The logic would eliminate all ijmāl → rebutted because where ijmāl is proven by evidence, preponderance does not apply. (3) Even if univocal, intending the common factor is impossible because the command specifies a particular cut location → rebutted by noting the dispute remains unresolved.
The final response indicates that the matter is still debated, but the Hanafi view is preponderant.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute has a direct fiqh impact: does the amputation of the hand involve cutting the whole arm to the shoulder (Hanafi) or only the hand to the wrist (other schools)?
The Hanafi ruling on the limit of amputation is derived from their understanding of 'yad'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (لا إجمال في اليد والقطع فلا إجمال في قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَاقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا ﴾ [المائدة: ۳۸] الحاصل أنه لا إجمال فيه باعتبار مفرداته في أنفسها (وشرذمة) قليلة قالوا (نعم) فيهما إجمال (فنعم) في الآية إجمال من جهتهما لنا اليد لغة للكل) إلى المنكب لصحة قولهم : بعض اليد والقطع للإبانة، ومنه سمي اليقين قطعاً لأنه يبين الخبر في العلم عن احتمال آخر المجملون قالوا اليد للكل إلى المنكب وإلى الكوع والقطع للإبانة والجرح والأصل الحقيقة) فيكون مشتركاً ولا قرينة فلزم الإجمال (قلنا : هما مجاز في الثانيين) اليد في الكوع والقطع في الجرح للتبادر في (الأولين ولا نسلم أصالة الحقيقة إذا تردد بين الاشتراك والحقيقة والمجاز واستدل كل من اليد والقطع ( يحتمل الاشتراك والتواطؤ والحقيقة والمجاز والإجمال على احتمال واحد دون اثنين لأن الدائر بين الحقيقي والمجازي لم يعد منه أي من الإجمال، وكذا الدائر بين فردي المتواطىء (فالعدم) أي عدم الإجمال (أغلب فهو المظنون، وأجيب أوّلاً كما في المختصر بأنه إثبات اللغة (بالترجيح وهو منهي عنه (أقول: قد تلقاه الناقدون بالقبول وهو ليس بشيء لأن المطلوب ههنا (نفي الإجمال، وهو ليس أمراً لغوياً بل (لازم) للكلام بلا) توقف على اللغة فلا يكون إثبات اللغة بالترجيح (نعم: لو قيل بعدم الاشتراك لرجحان عدم الإجمال بأن احتمال الاشتراك احتمال واحد مرجوح عند الاحتمالين (لتوجه) الجواب فتدبر ، و أجيب (ثانياً يلزم أن لا يكون (مجمل أصلاً (أبداً) فإن كل مجمل أنه يحتمل الاشتراك والتواطؤ والحقيقة والمجاز، ولا إجمال على الأخيرين بل على الأول فقط فعدم الإجمال راجح فلا إجمال ورد بأن ذلك أي الاستدلال برجحان عدم الإجمال على الإجمال (عند عدم الدليل الدال على الإجمال، وأما فيما ثبت الإجمال بدليل فلا يستدل بذلك على عدمه، فإن المظنة لا اعتبار لها عند وجود المثنة فافهم (و) أجيب (ثالثاً كما في التحرير نفي الإجمال على تقدير التواطىء ممنوع) فلم يكن عدم الإجمال أغلب (إذ إرادة القدر المشترك الذي وضع بإزائه المتواطىء لا يتصور، فإن الإطلاق منتف إجماعاً) إذ لا يقطع اليد من أي موضع كان بل من موضع معين (أقول وفيه أن النزاع مع قطع النظر عن الأمر يجري فيه )
Issue: There is no ijmāl in 'yad' and 'qat‘', so there is no ijmāl in His statement, the Exalted: 'And the male thief and the female thief, cut off their hands' [al-Mā’idah 5:38]. The summary is that there is no ijmāl in it considering its individual words in themselves. A small faction said: Yes, there is ijmāl in them, so yes, in the verse there is ijmāl from their perspective. For us: Yad linguistically [means] the whole [arm] to the shoulder, because their saying 'some of the hand' is valid; and qat‘ [means] severance, and from it certainty is called qat‘ because it clarifies the report in knowledge from other probability. The proponents of ijmāl said: Yad [means] the whole to the shoulder and to the wrist; and qat‘ [means] severance and wounding; and the principle is literal [meaning]; thus it is equivocal, and there is no indicator, so ijmāl is necessitated. We said: Both are metaphorical in the second meanings – yad for wrist and qat‘ for wound – because of immediate understanding in the first meanings, and we do not accept the principle of literal [meaning] when it is uncertain between equivocation, literal, and metaphorical. Each of yad and qat‘ may bear equivocation, univocality, literal, and metaphorical, and ijmāl is on one probability, not two, because the case oscillating between literal and metaphorical is not counted as ijmāl, nor is the case oscillating between two individuals of a univocal term. Thus non-ijmāl is more predominant, and it is the probable. And it was answered first, as in al-Mukhtaṣar, that this is establishing language by preponderance, which is forbidden. I say: The critics have accepted it, and it is nothing, because what is sought here is negation of ijmāl, which is not a linguistic matter but a necessary implication of speech, independent of language, so it is not establishing language by preponderance. Yes, if it were said that there is no equivocation due to the preponderance of non-ijmāl – because the probability of equivocation is one weak probability against two [others] – then the answer would be directed; so reflect. And it was answered second: It would necessitate that there be no ijmāl at all ever, because every mujmal can bear equivocation, univocality, literal, and metaphorical, and there is no ijmāl on the latter two but only on the first; thus non-ijmāl is preponderant, so no ijmāl. It was responded: This reasoning [using] preponderance of non-ijmāl over ijmāl is [only valid] when there is no evidence indicating ijmāl; but where ijmāl is proven by evidence, this reasoning does not prove its absence; for probability is not considered when certainty exists – understand. And it was answered third, as in al-Taḥrīr: Negation of ijmāl on the assumption of univocality is denied, so non-ijmāl is not more predominant, because intending the common factor for which a univocal term is placed is inconceivable, since unrestrictedness is rejected by consensus; for the hand is not cut from any place but from a specific place. I say: And in it, the dispute, disregarding the matter, runs within it.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage discusses whether the words 'يد' (hand) and 'قطع' (cutting) in the Quranic verse on theft (al-Mā’idah 5:38) are mujmal. The Hanafi position is that they are not. The primary linguistic meaning of 'يد' is the entire arm to the shoulder, as one can say 'some of the hand.' 'قطع' primarily means severance, from which 'قطعي' (certain) is derived because it cuts off other possibilities. A small group claims these words are equivocal: 'يد' could also mean the hand to the wrist, and 'قطع' could also mean wounding. The Hanafi response is that the secondary meanings are metaphorical, based on immediate understanding (tabādur). The argument over ijmāl involves probabilities: a word may be equivocal, univocal, literal, or metaphorical. Ijmāl only occurs in equivocation, not in the other categories. Since the words can be literal or metaphorical (two possibilities) and equivocal only one, non-ijmāl is preponderant. Three objections are addressed: (1) Using preponderance is establishing language by opinion, which is forbidden—rebutted because negating ijmāl is a necessary implication of speech, not a linguistic ruling. (2) The logic would eliminate all ijmāl—rebutted because where ijmāl is proven by evidence, preponderance does not apply. (3) Even if univocal, intending a common factor is impossible because the command specifies a particular place for cutting. The author notes that the dispute continues without resolving the matter.

Key Terms
اليد (al-yad): Linguistically, the entire arm to the shoulder; 'some of the hand' is valid. The wrist is metaphorical.
القطع (al-qat‘): Severance – dividing one part from another. Wounding is metaphorical.
تواطؤ (tawāṭu’): A word that is univocal (shared between individuals under a common genus) – here, the same word applied to different levels of the same meaning.
إجمال (ijmāl): Ambiguity in a word or phrase such that it cannot be understood without additional context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument in this text, why is the word 'يد' in the theft verse not mujmal despite having multiple possible meanings?
A) Because it is used metaphorically in all contexts
B) Because the primary meaning (whole arm) is immediately understood, and the other meanings are metaphorical
C) Because the word has only one meaning in the Quran
D) Because ijmāl requires the word to be equivocal and no determinative context

Key Takeaway
Immediate understanding (tabādur) of the primary meaning overrides potential ambiguity, preventing the word from being mujmal.

The Indication of Silence: Deriving the Father's Share from Quran 4:11
Proving silence as a valid legal indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of dalalat al-sukut to derive the father's inheritance share from Quran 4:11 when only parents are heirs.

Core Idea
Silence in a context of need is a valid legal indication (bayan).

In Usul al-Fiqh, not every ruling is derived from explicit text; sometimes silence carries legal weight. This lesson explores the concept of dalalat al-sukut (indication of silence) through the inheritance verse (4:11). The verse explicitly states the mother's share but is silent about the father's, yet the Hanafi school concludes that he takes the remainder. We'll walk through the reasoning and address the objection that this is mere implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source text and its explicit ruling
Locate Quran 4:11 and note that it explicitly assigns one-third to the mother when the deceased leaves only parents and no children or spouse. The father's share is not mentioned.
The verse reads: 'And his parents inherit him...'
2
Recognize the silence and its context
Understand that the text is silent about the father's share in a situation where a ruling is needed (place of need). According to Hanafi usul, silence in such a context functions as an implicit clarification (bayan).
The context: only the two parents are heirs; the mother's share is given, the father's is not.
3
Apply the principle of dalalat al-sukut
Apply the principle: 'السكوت في موضع الحاجة بيان' (silence in a place of need is a clarification). This indicates that the father receives the remainder, as he is the default asabah.
Customary necessity (al-luzum al-'urfi) makes this definitive.
4
Address the objection about implicative indication
Consider the objection that the remainder is simply a logical consequence (iltizam) of the mother's share and the exclusive heirship, not an indication of silence. The response: logical necessity is not absolute because the remainder could theoretically go to the public treasury; however, customarily it belongs to the father, and customary necessity suffices.
Thus, dalalat al-sukut is not the same as iltizam; it is a separate type of indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بيان الضرورة أقسام كلها دلالة (سكوت فالسكوت هناك دال منها ما يكون كالمنطوق في الوضوح (كقوله تعالى : ﴿وَوَرِثَهُ: أَبَوَاهُ ﴾ [النساء : ١١] فقط لا وارث ،آخر فإنه لو كان معه أحد الزوجين فليس للأم الثلث، بل ثلث الباقي : (( فلامه الثلث دل (السكوت عن نصيب الأب أن الباقي للأب لأن السكوت في موضع الحاجة بيان (وإن لم يعلم أنه عصبة) فيه دفع لما يوردان الأبوة يلزمه العصوبة والعصبة يأخذ الباقي فالدلالة من قبيل الإشارة، وقد يعترض بأن كون الثلث لأحدهما مع حصر الوارثة فيهما يلزمه كون الباقي للباقي منهما، فالدلالة التزامية لا دلالة السكوت، والجواب بمنع اللزوم، فأنه يجوز أن يكون الباقي مشتركاً بينه وبين بيت المال ساقط، فإن اللزوم العرفي كاف وههنا اللزوم في العرف قطعاً، ولا جواب إلا بأنه لا تنافي ساقط دلالة الالتزام ودلالة السكوت فتدبر
Then the clarification of necessity: all its types are indication. (Silence, for silence there is indicative, among them what is like the spoken in clarity, like His statement, Most High: "And his parents inherited him" [an-Nisa 11] only, no other heir. For if one of the spouses was with him, then the mother does not get a third, but rather a third of the remainder. So for the mother is a third, and silence about the father's share indicates that the remainder is for the father, because silence in a place of need is a clarification. And even if it is not known that he is an asaba, there is a refutation of what is raised: that fatherhood necessitates being an asaba, and the asaba takes the remainder. So the indication is from the category of hint. And it may be objected that the fact that a third is for one of them with the restriction of heirs to them necessitates that the remainder is for the other of them, so the indication is implicative, not the indication of silence. And the answer is by denying the necessity, for it is possible that the remainder is shared between him and the public treasury, but the customary necessity is sufficient, and here the necessity in custom is definite. And there is no answer except that there is no contradiction between the implicative indication and the indication of silence. So reflect.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage demonstrates how silence can serve as a legal indication (dalalah) in the absence of explicit speech. The author uses the inheritance verse from Surah an-Nisa (4:11) where Allah mentions the shares of parents but only explicitly states the mother's share when the deceased has no children. The text argues that the silence about the father's share indicates that he takes the remainder. An objection is raised: since the verse only mentions the two parents as heirs, the remainder necessarily goes to the other parent, so the indication is logical implication (iltizam) not silence. The author responds by denying that the implication is necessary, as the remainder could go to the public treasury, but customarily it is understood as belonging to the father. Thus, silence in a context of need functions as a clarification.

Key Terms
دلالة السكوت (dalalat al-sukut): The indication derived from silence in a context where speech is needed; it is considered a form of bayan.
السكوت في موضع الحاجة بيان (al-sukut fi mawdi' al-haja bayan): The principle that silence in a place of need constitutes a clarification.
دلالة الالتزام (dalalat al-iltizam): Implicative indication, where the meaning is derived from logical necessity of the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul principle demonstrated in this lesson, what does the silence about the father's share in Quran 4:11 indicate?
A) That the father takes nothing.
B) That the father takes the remainder because silence in a place of need is a clarification.
C) That the father's share is equal to the mother's.
D) That the matter is left to ijtihad and no ruling can be derived.

Key Takeaway
Silence in a place of need functions as a legal indication (dalalat al-sukut) and can yield a ruling without explicit text.

Indication of Silence: Deriving Khula as Talaq
Istinbat: deriving khula's nature from silence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the usul principle of silence (sukut) to derive a ruling from a Quranic text and contrast the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on khula.

Core Idea
Silence about the husband's action in the khula verse proves his act is talaq.

Can a legal ruling be derived from what a text does NOT say? In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi use of silence (sukut) as an indicator in the khula verse (2:229). By focusing on the absence of the husband's action, the Hanafi school concluded that khula is a form of talaq. We will trace this istinbat and contrast it with the Shafi'i view that khula is faskh (annulment).

Guided Path
1
Locate the revealed text
Read Quran 2:229. Note that the verse explicitly mentions the wife's ransom (iftida') but is silent about the husband's action. Identify the parties and the actions stated.
The verse: "...there is no blame on them for what she ransoms herself with."
2
Recognize the necessary missing element
For khula to dissolve the marriage, the husband must perform an act as well. The text does not say what he does, but an action is logically required.
Action is indispensable: the husband must do something to effect the separation.
3
Apply the principle of silence (sukut)
In Hanafi usul, when a text mentions one element and is silent about a necessary related element, the silence indicates that the missing element is the same as previously established. Here, the only previously mentioned marriage-dissolving act is talaq.
Al-sukutu yufidu al-ihalah 'ala al-ma'lum.
4
Derive the ruling
Therefore, the husband's necessary action must be talaq. Hence, khula itself is a type of talaq (divorce), not an independent annulment.
Final ruling: khula = talaq.
5
Confirm with practical consequences
If khula is talaq, the husband retains partial ownership during the wife's waiting period, and a subsequent remarriage limits him to the remaining divorces. This aligns with talaq rules and refutes the Shafi'i view.
These consequences validate the derivation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإشكال على الإمام فخر الإسلام بين مثال آخر: قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَحِلُّ لَكُمْ أَن تَأْخُذُوا مِمَّا اتَيْتُمُوهُنَّ شَيْئًا إِلَّا أَن يَخَافَا أَلَّا يُقِيمَا حُدُودَ اللَّهِ فَإِنْ خِفْتُمْ أَلا يُقِمَا حُدُودَ اللَّهِ فَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِمَا فِيمَا أَفْنَدَتْ بِهِ ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٢٩] فإنه تعالى لما بين فعل الزوجة حال الخلع وهو الافتداء وسكت عن فعل الزوج، ولا بد من فعله، فعلم أن فعله هو المذكور سابقاً وهو الطلاق، فعلم أن الخلع طلاق لا كما زعم الإمام الشافعي في القولين أنه فسخ لا طلاق، حتى لو تزوج إياها بعد الخلع من غير تحلل الزوج الآخر لا يملك إلا طلاقين خلافاً له، ولما صار طلاقاً وفي الطلاق يبقى نوع ملك إلى انقضاء العدة يقع الطلاق بعد الخلع في العدة، فيلحق المختلعة الطلاق خلافاً له، وقد بين الإمام فخر صريح الإسلام هذه الفريعة ببيان أطول
And the problem for Imam Fakhr al-Islam is another example: Allah Most High said: "And it is not lawful for you to take anything from what you have given them unless they both fear that they cannot uphold the limits of Allah. If you fear that they cannot uphold the limits of Allah, then there is no blame on them for what she ransoms herself with" [al-Baqarah 2:229]. For He, Most High, when He explained the wife's action in khula, which is ransom, and remained silent about the husband's action, and it is inevitable that he acts, so it is known that his action is what was mentioned previously, namely divorce. So it is known that khula is divorce, not as Imam al-Shafi'i claimed in the two sayings that it is annulment, not divorce, so much so that if he marries her after khula without the other husband's dissolution, he only has two divorces left, contrary to him. And since it became divorce, and in divorce a type of ownership remains until the end of the waiting period, divorce after khula occurs during the waiting period, so divorce attaches to the woman who has been khula'd, contrary to him. And Imam Fakhr al-Sari' al-Islam has explained this subsidiary issue with a longer explanation.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage uses the khula verse (2:229) to demonstrate the indication of silence. The verse mentions the wife's action (ransom/khula) but is silent about the husband's action. Since the husband must perform some act to effect the separation, and the only previously mentioned act that can dissolve a marriage is divorce (talaq), the silence indicates that his act is talaq. This establishes the Hanafi ruling that khula is a form of talaq (divorce), not faskh (annulment) as held by Imam al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi position has practical consequences: after khula, the husband retains some marital authority during the waiting period, and if he marries her again later, he has only two remaining divorces. The text refutes the Shafi'i view that khula is annulment.

Key Terms
خلع (khula'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for a payment (ransom).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The verse explicitly says 'talaq'.
B) The verse describes the wife's ransom and is silent about the husband's act; silence indicates the husband's necessary act is talaq.
C) The verse prohibits all forms of khula except talaq.
D) The verse allows the husband to take back the ransom.

Key Takeaway
Khula is a form of talaq, derived from the Quranic silence about the husband's necessary action, with clear practical consequences.
