مفيدة للقطع (وقيل) ذلك العدد (خمسة قياساً على اللعان) فإنه خمس شهادات، وإذا قبل إخبار رجل خمس مرات وأفاد اليقين فإخبار خمسة رجال بالطريق الأولى (وقطع القاضي) الباقلاني بنفي الأربعة، إذ لو (أفاد خبر الأربعة اليقين لم تحتج شهود الزنا إلى التزكية) لأن العدالة غير معتبرة في التواتر وفيه تأمل ويرد عليه وروداً ظاهراً أن التزكية في الشهادة أمر تعبدي لا لتحصيل اليقين، ألا ترى أن سبعين ألفاً لو شهدوا بالزنا لوجب التزكية أيضاً، لذا لو حصل اليقين لا عن شهود لم يجب الحد ولذا لم يحدّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم تلك المرأة وقال: «لو رجمت من غير شهود لرجمت هذه» رواه البخاري فإن قلت غاية ما لزم من دليله عدم إفادة الأربعة في الزنا، ولا يلزم منه عدم الإفادة في صورة أخرى قال : (وذلك) أي دليل القاضي بناء على ما قاله هو ووافقه أبو الحسين) من المعتزلة أن كل عدد أفاد علماً بواقعة لشخص فمثله أي مثل هذا العدد يفيد العلم) (بغير تلك الواقعة لشخص آخر) فلو كان الأربعة مفيداً للعلم في الواقعة لأفاد في الزنا، فلا يحتاج إلى التزكية (وفيه وما فيه) فإنه باطل بالضرورة، كيف والإفادة تختلف باختلاف المخبر عنه والمخبرين وغير ذلك، وربما يؤوّل بأن كل عدد أفاد علماً بواقعة فمثل هذا العدد في الأحوال العارضة لها، وعلى هذا لا يكفي في دفع الإيراد، فإنه لما كان مشاهدة الزنا بعيداً في العادة لكونه في الأكثر في مكان خال لم تفد الأربعة ويجوز أن تفيد في غيره مما تسهل فيه المشاهدة فتدبر ( وتردد) القاضي (في الخمسة) ولم يقطع بانتفائه (ويرد عليه أن وجوب التزكية مشترك بين الأربعة والخمسة إذا شهدوا بالزنا، فيجب أن لا تفيد العلم أيضاً، بل يجب أن لا يتحقق تواتر أصلاً، فإن كل عدد لو شهدوا بالزنا وجب تزكيتهم (إلا) أن يقول القاضي حال كونه فارقاً) بين الصورتين كل خمسة صادقة) قد تفيد العلم) بما أخبروا (فإذا لم تفد) العلم (في الزنا علم أن فيهم كذوباً أي من شأنه أن يكذب، لكنه غير معلوم بالتعين لا أن فيهم كذوباً في الأخبار حتى يقال : كذب واحد يستلزم كذب الكل، لأن كلامهم واحد فالتزكية تعلم صدق الباقي وهو النصاب فلم يلزم منه أن أخبار الخمسة الصادقة غير مفيد بل بقي الاحتمال كما كان بخلاف (الأربعة فإن التزكية فيهم ليس لهذا فإنه إذا علم كذوبية واحد لم يبق الباقي نصاباً فلا تفيد التزكية والتزكية واجبة قطعاً، فليس إلا لأنّ الأربعة غير مفيدة للعلم قطعاً، فاتضح الفرق فتدبر) فإنه غاية ما يوجه به كلام القاضي، لكن بقي فيه شيء، فإنه قد مر أن كل عدد يفيد العلم في واقعة مفيد في جميع الوقائع، فلو الخمسة في واقعة لأفاد في الزنا أيضاً وحينئذ لا تصح التزكية فإذا لم تفد لم يعلم أن فيهم كذوباً بل علم أنه لا يفيد في واقعة أصلاً، وكونه من شأنه أن يكذب لا ينافي حصول العلم، فإن العدالة غير معتبرة في التواتر فافهم (وقيل) أقل العدد المعتبر (سبعة قياساً على غسل الإناء من ولوغ الكلب سبع مرات قال النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: «إذا شرب الكلب في إناء أحدكم فليغسله سبعاً رواه البخاري، وهذا الحديث منسوخ عندنا بحديث الاكتفاء بالثلاث في غسل النجاسات، كما بين في موضعه وجه القياس أن للسبعة تأثيراً في التطهير، واليقين أيضاً تطهير فتأمل فإن فيه ما فيه (وقيل) أقل العدد المشروط في التواتر (عشرة لقوله تعالى : ﴿ تِلْكَ عَشَرَةٌ كَامِلَةٌ [البقرة : ١٩٦] حيث وصف العشرة بالكمال فيكون مفيداً للعلم (وقيل) أقله اثنا عشر عدد نقباء بني إسرائيل حيث جعلهم موسى على نبينا وعليه الصلاة والسلام أمناء وأرسلهم ليعرفوا من أخبار الجبابرة ولولا أن خبرهم مفيد للعلم لما بعثهم لذلك (وقيل) أقله (عشرون ، قال تعالى: ﴿إِن يَكُن مِّنكُمْ عِشْرُونَ صَبِرُونَ يَغْلِبُوا مِائَتَيْنِ [الأنفال : ٦٥]) حيث فرض عليهم الجهاد لما كان خبرهم بمجيء الرسول، وإيجابه الأيمان مفيداً للعلم، حتى وجب قتالهم بالمخالفة عنهم (وقيل) أقله (أربعون ) قال عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام: (خير السرايا أربعون وليست الخيرية إلا لأن خبرهم مفيد للعلم حتى وجب القتال بمخالفتهم (وقيل) أقله (خمسون قياساً على القسامة فإن فيها أخبار خمسين رجلاً إنهم ما قتلوا وما عرفوا قاتلاً، فتخصيص الخمسين إنما هو لكون خبرهم مفيداً للعلم دون الأقل منهم (وقيل) أقله سبعون لاختيار (موسى) على نبينا وعليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام سبعين رجلاً لميقاته حتى يسمعوا كلام الله تعالى ويخبروا من وراءهم، فلولا خبرهم مفيد للعلم لاختار أكثر ، ولو كان خبر الأقل مفيداً لاكتفى بهم (وقيل) أقله (أزيد من ثلثمائة
For if they report a report that they sensed it, their knowledge necessarily follows. I say: the intended meaning of reliance on sense is that the report is about sensed matters, not that they reported that they sensed it. So there is no sufficiency, so reflect. (It was said) in the marginalia of Mirzajan: if the condition of the necessary condition were sufficient for the condition of the necessary, as Ibn al-Hajib said, then the condition of the first (that the reporters reach a number that prevents collusion on falsehood) would make the last two (that they are such in every generation and reliance on sense) unnecessary. Because if the number of reporters reaches a limit that prevents the mind from agreeing on falsehood, that only happens in sensed matters, for in intellectual matters it is not impossible to agree on falsehood. And the middle and ends must be equal, otherwise agreement would be possible. But the sufficiency of the condition of the necessary for the necessary is established, so the negation of the condition of the last two follows. Or it is said: the sufficiency of the first for the last two is false, so the sufficiency of the necessary for the necessary is false. Then the last is needed even if the first entails it. On the first interpretation, it is an objection to the majority like Ibn al-Hajib's objection; on the second, it is a response to Ibn al-Hajib's objection. I say: this statement is not valid regardless, rather the intended meaning of the first condition is the existence of the number in the mentioned limit in some generation. As for its existence in all generations, that is from the third condition, and there is no doubt that the first does not entail this. And the intended meaning of 'preventing the mind' from collusion on falsehood is its prevention after the existence of the other conditions. That is, the intended meaning is the gathering of the number in terms of multitude, even if this prevention occurs after other conditions are realized, so that preventing collusion does not need a larger number than it. And the intended meaning is not the prevention of collusion at the moment, such that it would be objected that this includes the other conditions, so it is entailing them. Then it becomes clear that the first is not entailing the last two. This is how this point should be understood. Then they differed on the minimum number stipulated in tawatur. It was said: four, by analogy to witnesses of adultery, for it is a great matter and we are commanded to avoid doubts, and there is no doubt that non-mutawatir involves doubt, so it is known that four produce certainty. And it was said: that number is five, by analogy to li'an, for it is five testimonies, and if the report of one man five times is accepted and produces certainty, then the report of five men is even more apt. And al-Qadi al-Baqillani decisively negated four, because if the report of four produced certainty, the witnesses of adultery would not need tazkiyah (validation of character), since justice is not considered in tawatur. In this there is consideration. And it is objected to him manifestly that tazkiyah in testimony is a devotional matter, not for obtaining certainty. Do you not see that if seventy thousand testified to adultery, tazkiyah would still be obligatory. Hence, if certainty is obtained not from witnesses, the hadd penalty is not obligatory. Hence, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him and his family and companions) did not stone that woman and said: 'If I were to stone without witnesses, I would have stoned this one.' Reported by al-Bukhari. If you say: the utmost of his proof is that four do not produce certainty in adultery, and it does not imply that they do not produce certainty in another case, he said: 'That is, the proof of al-Qadi is based on what he said, and Abu al-Husayn from the Mu'tazila agreed with him, that every number that gives knowledge of an incident to a person, then the same number gives knowledge of a different incident to another person.' So if four gave knowledge of an incident, they would give knowledge in adultery, so tazkiyah would not be needed. In this there is much to say, for it is invalid necessarily. How so? Because giving knowledge differs according to the reported matter, the reporters, and other factors. Perhaps it is interpreted that every number that gives knowledge of an incident gives knowledge of the same type of incident in incidental matters. Even so, this does not suffice to repel the objection, because since witnessing adultery is usually rare, often in a secluded place, four do not give knowledge, but they might give knowledge in other matters where witnessing is easier. So ponder. And al-Qadi was hesitant about five and did not decisively negate it. And it is objected to him that the obligation of tazkiyah is common between four and five if they testify to adultery, so they should also not give knowledge. Rather, tawatur should not occur at all, because every number, if they testify to adultery, must be validated, unless al-Qadi says, distinguishing between the two cases: 'Every truthful five might give knowledge of what they reported. So if they do not give knowledge in adultery, it is known that among them is a liar, i.e., someone who is prone to lie, but not specifically known, not that among them is a liar in the reports such that it would be said: one lie entails the lie of all, because their speech is one. So tazkiyah informs of the truthfulness of the rest, who constitute the required number. So it does not follow from this that the report of truthful five is not beneficial; rather, the probability remains as it was. Unlike four, for tazkiyah with them is not for this reason, because if the lying of one is known, the rest do not constitute the required number, so tazkiyah is not beneficial. And tazkiyah is absolutely obligatory, so it is only because four do not produce knowledge absolutely. So the difference is clear, ponder.' This is the utmost by which the words of al-Qadi are directed. But there remains something: it has been mentioned that every number that gives knowledge in one incident gives knowledge in all incidents. So if five give knowledge in one incident, they would give knowledge in adultery also. Then tazkiyah would not be valid. So if they do not give knowledge, it is not known that there is a liar among them; rather, it is known that they do not give knowledge in any incident at all. And being prone to lie does not prevent the attainment of knowledge, for justice is not considered in tawatur. So understand. And it was said: the minimum number considered is seven, by analogy to washing a vessel from a dog's licking seven times. The Prophet (peace be upon him and his family and companions) said: 'If a dog drinks from the vessel of one of you, let him wash it seven times.' Reported by al-Bukhari. This hadith is abrogated in our view by the hadith of sufficing with three in washing impurities, as explained in its place. The point of analogy is that seven has an effect in purification, and certainty is also purification. So reflect, for there is much in it. And it was said: the minimum number stipulated in tawatur is ten, due to the Almighty's saying: 'Those are ten complete' (Quran 2:196) where He described ten as complete, so it gives knowledge. And it was said: its minimum is twelve, the number of the chiefs of the Children of Israel, for Moses (peace be upon our Prophet and upon him) made them trustees and sent them to learn news of the tyrants. Had their report not given knowledge, he would not have sent them for that. And it was said: its minimum is twenty. The Almighty said: 'If there are among you twenty steadfast, they will overcome two hundred' (Quran 8:65) where He enjoined jihad upon them because their report of the arrival of the messenger and the imposition of oaths gave knowledge, such that fighting them was obligatory when they opposed. And it was said: its minimum is forty. He (peace be upon him and upon his family and companions) said: 'The best of expeditions is forty.' The best is only because their report gives knowledge, such that fighting was obligatory when they opposed. And it was said: its minimum is fifty, by analogy to the qasamah (oath of fifty), for in it fifty men swear that they did not kill and do not know the killer. The specification of fifty is because their report gives knowledge, unlike fewer. And it was said: its minimum is seventy, because Moses (peace be upon our Prophet and upon him and upon his family and companions) chose seventy men for his appointment to hear the speech of Allah and inform those behind them. Had their report not given knowledge, he would have chosen more. And if the report of fewer gave knowledge, he would have sufficed with them. And it was said: its minimum is more than three hundred.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for tawatur (mutawatir transmission) and the disputed minimum number of transmitters required to produce certain knowledge. The commentator, Abd al-Ali al-Ansari, begins by analyzing a claim made by Ibn al-Hajib and the marginalia of Mirzajan that the condition of the number in one generation (the first condition) is sufficient to dispense with the conditions that this number be present in every generation (the second condition) and that the report be based on sense perception (the third condition). The commentator refutes this, explaining that the first condition refers only to the number in a single generation, not that it guarantees the other conditions. He then clarifies that the prevention of collusion on falsehood only occurs after all other conditions are met. The passage then transitions to the disagreement over the minimum number for tawatur. The text lists and evaluates various opinions: four (by analogy to witnesses of adultery), five (by analogy to li'an), seven (by analogy to washing a vessel from a dog's licking, though this hadith is considered abrogated in the Hanafi school), ten (quoting Q. 2:196 'those are ten complete'), twelve (the number of Israelite chiefs), twenty (from Q. 8:65), forty (from a hadith about the best expedition), fifty (by analogy to qasamah), seventy (from the story of Moses), and more than three hundred (from a hadith about the number of Companions at the pledge of Ridwan). The commentator provides critical analysis of al-Qadi al-Baqillani's position on four and five, showing the weaknesses in his reasoning. The passage does not explicitly adopt any one number, but the critical remarks suggest that the number is not fixed; rather, the condition is that the number must be such that collusion on falsehood is impossible, which may vary.

Key Terms
تواتر (tawātur): A report transmitted by such a large number of narrators in each generation that it is impossible for them to have colluded on a falsehood, thus producing certain knowledge.
عدد التواتر (ʿadad al-tawātur): The minimum number of transmitters required for a report to be considered mutawatir. The text lists various opinions from 4 to over 300.
تزكية (tazkiyah): Validation of a witness's character (ʿadālah) in legal proceedings. In the context of tawatur, the commentator argues it is a devotional requirement, not needed for epistemic certainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the opinion that the minimum number for tawatur is ten is based on which evidence?
A) Analogy to witnesses of adultery
B) Analogy to li'an
C) The Quranic verse 'Those are ten complete' (2:196)
D) The hadith about forty expeditions

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not adopt a fixed number for tawatur; the condition is the impossibility of collusion, which varies by context.

Tawatur Ma'nawi: Certainty from Collective Meaning
Defining and exemplifying tawatur ma'nawi as a source of certain knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tawatur ma'nawi, identify its conditions, and apply it to derive certain knowledge of attributes from multiple ahad reports.

Core Idea
Many weak reports can collectively produce certainty about a shared meaning — that is tawatur ma'nawi.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut explains how tawatur ma'nawi works: a multitude of ahad reports converge on a common meaning, leading to intuitive certainty. It is a key concept in Hanafi usul because it allows us to accept historically established virtues and events even when individual reports are not mutawatir. We will dissect its definition, conditions, and examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term
The passage defines 'al-tawatur al-ma'nawi'. Locate the term and note its meaning: the accumulation of solitary reports that agree on a meaning (explicit or implicit).
The definition includes the phrase 'wa law iltizaman' meaning even if the meaning is implicit.
2
Understand the condition
The condition is that each report individually is ahad (solitary), but collectively they share a common meaning. The passage states this explicitly: 'kathrat al-ahad al-muttafiqah fi ma'nan'.
The common meaning can be implicit (iltizami), not necessarily the literal wording.
3
Grasp the epistemological status
The passage asserts that this creates certain knowledge (ilm yaqini) that is intuitive (daruri), known by introspection (wijdan), and does not require external proof. Deniers are dismissed by reason.
This is a key Hanafi position: tawatur ma'nawi produces certainty just like tawatur lafzi.
4
Examine the examples
The passage lists examples: reports about Hatim's generosity, Ali's bravery, Umar's justice, Abu Dharr's asceticism, and the sainthood of Abd al-Qadir al-Jilani. None of these individual reports are mutawatir, but collectively they yield certain knowledge of the attribute.
The exception: some of al-Jilani's miracles are also mutawatir lafzi.
5
Recognize the contrast
Tawatur ma'nawi differs from tawatur lafzi (verbatim tawatur) in that certainty is about the shared meaning, not the exact wording. Both produce certain but different types of knowledge.
Hanafis use this to establish the virtues of the Salaf and saints without requiring each story to be rigorously verified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: كثرة الآحاد المتفقة في معنى ولو التزاماً أي ولو كان المعنى التزامياً (توجب العلم بالقدر المشترك بين تلك الآحاد ولا يحتاج إلى ذلك إلى الدليل، لأن هذا العلم ضروري يعلم تحققه عند الرجوع إلى الوجدان ولو وجد منكر لا يلتفت إليه ويكذب ببداهة العقل (وهو التواتر المعنوي في الاصطلاح وذلك كوقائع حاتم في عطاياه، و وقائع أمير المؤمنين (علي) رضي الله عنه في حروبه) ووقائع أمير المؤمنين عمر رضي الله عنه في عدله وجلادته في الدين ووقائع أبي ذر رضي الله عنه في زهده إلى غير ذلك من أخبار الصحابة والتابعين وغيرهم وككرامات قطب الأقطاب الملة والدين عبد القادر الجيلاني قدس سره العزيز (فليعلم السخاوة والشجاعة والعدل والزهد والولاية والكرامة وغيرها (مع أن شيئاً تلك الجزئيات لم يتواتر) بخصوصه إلا شيئاً قليلاً من كرامات قطب الأقطاب، فإنها وجدت متواترة اللفظ أيضاً
Issue: The abundance of solitary reports that agree on a meaning, even if the meaning is implicit, creates certain knowledge of the common element among those reports, and no proof is needed for that, because this knowledge is intuitive, realized through introspection. Even if a denier is found, he is not heeded and is dismissed by the dictates of reason. This is called tawatur ma'nawi in terminology. Examples: the incidents of Hatim in his generosity, the incidents of the Commander of the Faithful (Ali) in his battles, the incidents of the Commander of the Faithful Umar in his justice and firmness in religion, the incidents of Abu Dharr in his asceticism, and so on from the reports of the Companions, Successors, and others, and the miracles of the Qutb al-Aqtab Abd al-Qadir al-Jilani (may Allah sanctify his noble secret). So generosity, bravery, justice, asceticism, sainthood, miracles, etc. are known, even though none of those particular incidents are individually mutawatir, except a few of the miracles of the Qutb al-Aqtab, which are found to be mutawatir also in wording.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines tawatur ma'nawi: when many solitary reports (ahad) all point to a common meaning (explicit or implicit), the collective agreement produces certain knowledge of that meaning. For example, each report individually may be weak, but the multitude of reports about Hatim's generosity lead to certain knowledge that he was generous. Similarly, stories of Ali's bravery, Umar's justice, Abu Dharr's asceticism, and the sainthood of Abd al-Qadir al-Jilani are known through meaning-based tawatur. This knowledge is intuitive and does not require external proof. The only exception is that some of al-Jilani's miracles are also mutawatir in wording (tawatur lafzi). Thus, the concept allows us to accept historically established virtues and events without requiring each specific report to be mutawatir.

Key Terms
التواتر المعنوي (al-tawatur al-ma'nawi): The accumulation of many solitary reports that agree on a common meaning, yielding certain knowledge of that meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the epistemological status of the knowledge produced by tawatur ma'nawi?
A) It is speculative and requires external proof
B) It is intuitive and does not need proof
C) It is weaker than a single ahad report
D) It only applies to miracles of saints

Key Takeaway
Tawatur ma'nawi transforms multiple solitary reports into certain knowledge of a shared attribute without needing external proof.

Sufficiency of the Quran's Miracle and Tawatur of the Moon-Splitting
Defending the Quran's i'jaz and arguing tawatur of the moon-splitting miracle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi argument that the Quran's tawatur and continued i'jaz suffice as proof, and explain why the moon-splitting miracle is considered mutawatir.

Core Idea
The Quran's continuous transmission and unimpeached i'jaz make it a self-sufficient miracle.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut defends the Quran as the primary proof for prophethood, addressing objections that other miracles need separate tawatur. The author argues that the Quran's mutawatir transmission and the fact that it was never successfully challenged establish its miraculous nature. The moon-splitting miracle is cited as a key example of an event whose tawatur is established through the Quran itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
The author asserts that the Quran's miracle alone is sufficient proof for prophethood; other miracles do not need independent tawatur because the Quran's i'jaz is continuous and self-evident.
This is the main thesis of the passage.
2
Understand the objection
An objection is raised: not everyone recognizes the Quran's i'jaz (since it requires knowledge of Arabic eloquence), so other miracles are needed to convince the general public.
This objection is attributed to Mirzajan in the commentary.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the Quran's transmission is mutawatir, and the fact that no one has ever produced a challenge to it despite intense opposition is itself a publicly known miracle. Thus, it suffices.
The author also mentions that the common element among all miracles (their miraculous nature) is mutawatir even if individual reports are ahad.
4
Examine the moon-splitting example
The author cites the splitting of the moon (Quran 54:1-2) as a miracle whose tawatur is established by the Quran itself. Since the Quran is mutawatir, the event it reports is also proven with certainty.
This addresses the objection that only a few witnesses saw the event.
5
Apply the principle to other miracles
The author concludes that the Quran renders other miracles unnecessary for proof, though they may still be accepted if transmitted mutawatir. The branches discussed are not of the type that motivate widespread transmission.
The issue of khabar al-wahid in general calamities is deferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني/ السنة إنهم يقرون أيضاً أن أمير المؤمنين أشجع الناس وأن أهل بيته كلهم كانوا ناصريه، وأن مثل عمار والمقداد وأبي ذر أيضاً كانوا من ناصريه وكان أبو ذر ذا قبيلة ولم يخف من أمثال أبي جهل حين أظهر الإسلام بين أعينهم، وإذا كان خائفاً مع وجود الناصرين فأين الأشجعية؟ هو بل هذا الخوف مناف للشجاعة ومثبت لأشجعية الخلفاء الثلاثة وجلادتهم، فقد بان لك بأقوم الحجج أن مذهب الشيعة الشنيعة لا يختاره إلا سفيه انتهى إلى حدة البلادة ومفض إلى أمور مستشنعة وأما كلام عيسى) في المهد (والمعجزات المذكورة (فلو كثر مشاهدوها لتواترت كما قيل في انشقاق القمر وحنين (الجذع إنهما متواترتان وصرح بتواترهما السبكي، فلم ينقله إلا من شاهد من الصحابة، فليس هذا من الباب في شيء (على أن القرآن مغنِ) عن سائر المعجزات، فليس هناك على نقل المعجزات الأخر دواع، فليس من الباب في شيء (قيل) في حواشي ميرزاجان»: التحقيق أن إعجازه لكمال البلاغة) بحيث لا يقدر البشر على إتيان مثله في البلاغة فلا يعلمه إلا الأفراد من البلغاء الذين لم يوجدوا في كل عصر ولا أقل من أنهم قلوا غاية القلة (فكون القرآن مستمراً لا يغني عن ذكر تلك المعجزات) لعدم علم الأكثر بإعجاز ، ولا بد من نقل معجز يعلم إعجازه ليقوم حجة، والجواب عنه أنه نقل القرآن تواتراً، ونقل إنه لم يعارض مع جد المخالفين في ذلك تواتراً، وهذا القدر كاف للعلم بالإعجاز، ويقوم حجة، فنقله على هذا الوجه يكون مغنياً (أقول) في الجواب (البلاغة صفة لازمة له فما دام موجوداً معجز، وفي ذكر المعجز الموجود كفاية) عن ذكر معجزاً آخر قد زال (لا ريب فيه) وفيه نظر ظاهر، فإن الشاك إنما شكك بأن هذا المعجز الموجود لا ينتفع به الأكثر لعدم علمهم بإعجازه، فذكره لا يغني عن ذكر المعجزات الأخر التي إعجازها بين عند كل أحد، فيقوم حجة، فينتفع به فلا جواب إلا بالمراجعة إلى ما قلنا، ويمكن الجواب أيضاً بأن نقل كل معجزة معجزة سوى القرآن وإن كان أحادياً، لكن القدر المشترك بين الكل متواتر، وهو يقوم حجة، فنقله كفاية عن نقل واحد واحد (وأما الفروع التي استدلوا بها فليست مما تتوفر الدواعي على نقله مطلقاً) والمسألة وكانت مفروضة فيما تكثر مشاهدوها وتوفرت الدواعي على نقلهم إياه، وأما الكلام بأن خبر الواحد لا يقبل فيما عمت البلوى به فكلام آخر سيتضح في المسألة الآتية .
[This passage begins with a refutation of the Shi'a view of bravery, then discusses the miracles of Jesus, the splitting of the moon, and the sufficiency of the Quran's miracle. It states that if the observers of a miracle were many, it would be mutawatir; the splitting of the moon is mentioned in the Quran, so no other transmission is needed. Some objected that the Quran's eloquence is not known to all, so other miracles are needed; the reply is that the Quran's transmission is tawatur, and that is sufficient. The common element among all miracles is mutawatir. The branches they adduced are not such that there is ample motive to transmit them.]
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an extended usul analysis defending the sufficiency of the Quran as a miracle and clarifying the conditions for tawatur. The author begins by refuting a Shi'a claim about bravery, then transitions to the issue of miracles. The key point: if a miracle had many witnesses, it would be mutawatir; the splitting of the moon is mentioned in the Quran, so no separate tawatur is needed. However, some object that not everyone recognizes the Quran's i'jaz, so other miracles are needed. The author responds that the Quran's tawatur and the fact it was never challenged suffice. The passage ends by noting that the branches under discussion are not such that there is ample motive to transmit them, and that the issue of khabar al-wahid in general calamities will be clarified in the next mas'alah.

Key Terms
تواتر (tawātur): A narration transmitted by so many narrators that collusion on a lie is impossible, yielding certain knowledge.
إعجاز (iʿjāz): The miraculous inimitability of the Quran, proving its divine origin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is the Quran's miracle sufficient for proving prophethood without the need for other miracles to be mutawatir?
A) Because the Quran is the only miracle that occurred.
B) Because the Quran's transmission is mutawatir and it was never successfully challenged.
C) Because the moon-splitting miracle is mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because the companions saw the Quran's miracle.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's mutawatir transmission and its ongoing inimitability constitute a self-sufficient proof for prophethood, making separate tawatur of other miracles unnecessary.

Al-Quran as Source
intermediate
8 min
Surah al-Tawbah 9:122 as Proof for Khabar Wahid
Analysis of verse 9:122 as evidence for wajib al-akhdh bi khabar al-wahid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that 'ta'ifa' is khass (specific) and derive the obligation to act upon khabar wahid from Surah al-Tawbah 9:122.

Core Idea
Surah 9:122 proves the report of one person obligates action – 'ta'ifa' includes one, and warning without obligation is futile.

One of the strongest proofs for the authority of solitary reports (khabar wahid) in Hanafi usul is derived from Surah al-Tawbah 9:122. This verse commands a group to go forth to gain understanding and warn their people so that they may beware. The Hanafi school argues that the term 'ta'ifa' includes a single individual, and the purpose of warning implies an obligation to act upon the warning. This lesson unpacks the linguistic argument, the refutation of objections, and how the verse interacts with consensus to establish a definitive ruling.

Guided Path
1
Locate the verse and its key terms
Identify Surah al-Tawbah 9:122. Focus on the words 'ta'ifa' (a party/group), 'liyunziru' (to warn), and 'la‘allahum yahdharun' (that they may beware). These are the operative terms for deriving the obligation.
The full verse is quoted in the source.
2
Classify 'ta'ifa' linguistically
Determine whether 'ta'ifa' is 'amm (general) or khass (specific). The Hanafi position, following Ibn ‘Abbas, holds that 'ta'ifa' includes even one person, making it khass and thus qat‘i (definitive) in meaning.
Contrast with the Shafi‘i view that 'ta'ifa' is general.
3
Analyze the implication of 'la‘allahum yahdharun'
The verse states the purpose of warning is that they may beware. Bewaring (hadhar) implies taking precautionary action, which logically requires acting upon the warning. If acting were not obligatory, the warning would be futile (la yukhalla 'an al-fa'ida).
This is a mafhum (implied meaning) argument.
4
Address the objection of restriction to fatwa
Some opponents restrict 'indhar' (warning) to fatwa for the common people, excluding riwaya (narration) for mujtahids. The Hanafi response: this restriction is arbitrary (tahakkum); the verse’s apparent meaning is absolute, covering both fatwa and narration.
The passage calls this 'takhsis bi al-tahakkum'.
5
Evaluate the consensus argument about zann
A second refutation uses consensus that zann from a qat‘i al-matan text must be followed. The verse generates a zann of obligation for khabar wahid. However, this argument is weak because it would make all furu‘ issues qat‘i, which is not accepted. The stronger method is combining the verse with consensus to produce qat‘ specifically about the obligation to act.
This is the novel verification by Muttali‘ al-Asrar.

Source Text

And fourthly, the statement of the Most High: 'Then why not from every group of them a party go forth... until His words: that they may beware' (al-Tawbah 9:122). It means: Why not from every section of them a group go forth to gain understanding in religion and to warn their people when they return to them, that they may beware? For indeed warning only comes from what is obligatory, and the verse indicates warning, so acting according to the reports of the group is obligatory, and the group from each section does not reach the level of tawatur. Rather, the group, according to what Ibn 'Abbas (may God be pleased with him) said, includes the one and the multitude. And it can be established that the verse indicates that the going forth of the group for warning by reporting is definitely obligatory. If acting upon it were not obligatory, the warning would be devoid of benefit. And it may be said against it: Perhaps the warning from each group is so that knowledge is obtained by warning after warning upon reaching the level of tawatur. And it is answered that this is contrary to the apparent meaning, for the verse indicates the warning by which caution is obtained, so it does not wait for the number of tawatur. And this inference was deemed unlikely because (the intended meaning of warning is fatwa for the general public, not narration of hadith for the mujtahid, which is outside the dispute. Even if it is granted that the intended meaning is narration, then (the apparent meaning) i.e., the mentioned verse is apparent, and it (by its conjectural nature) is not sufficient here because the issue is usul. And the unlikeliness is refuted by (the fact that specifying to fatwa is arbitrary, rather the apparent meaning is warning absolutely, for the general public by fatwa and for the specialists by narrating hadith. And the general word is definitive, so there is no conjecturality. Rather, there is no generality here, for 'ta'ifa' is absolute and it is from the specific, and the specific is definitively agreed upon. This is only valid if they suffice with definitiveness in the broader sense in usul, as is apparent. So understand and be upright. And the unlikeliness may be refuted secondly by consensus on the obligation of following conjecture, meaning that the verse generated a conjecture of obligation to act according to a solitary report, and definitive consensus indicates the obligation to act according to the conjecture generated by the Law. So acting according to the report becomes obligatory definitively. So it is not said that then it is enough to say: The report is useful for conjecture and it is obligatory to act by consensus, so the appeal to the verse becomes superfluous. And this (i.e., this refutation) is weak, because the one who does not suffice with conjecture in usul does not suffice with the general evidence in it either, due to its application in furu'. For example, if it is said: The obligation of witr is conjectured, and conjecture is obligatory to follow, then all furu' would become definitive. So this general evidence is not sufficient here; rather, definitive evidence is needed for each individual issue. And it is not clear, based on the foundation that conjecture is not considered, a justification, unless it is said: The general evidence does not produce definitiveness, otherwise it would produce conjecturality in furu' as well. So it is not sufficient for those who require definitiveness in usul. As I say: Moreover, the opponent denies consensus on following conjecture absolutely, but rather on what is definitive in text (qat'i al-matan) but conjectural in indication (dhanni al-dalala). And the conjecture generated from a definitive text is obligatory to follow by consensus, while following the conjecture generated from a conjectural text is disputed. So understand. And for the author of Muttali' al-Asrar (may his secret be sanctified) there is a novel verification: that the general evidence when combined with the detailed evidence, if it yields definitiveness, must be considered. Why not? Otherwise, existing knowledge would be wasted. And here it yields because the verse requires obligation to act upon the conjectural report conjecturally, and consensus has made acting upon this conjecture obtained from a definitive text definitively obligatory, so acting upon the report becomes definitively obligatory. As for furu', if by this evidence we prove definitively the obligation to act, then there is no disgrace in committing to it, for acting upon it is definitively obligatory. If what is intended is definitiveness of the furu' themselves, then this consensus does not benefit it. And here the required is the obligation to act, and it is necessary. And by this, the two doubts are refuted. Moreover, what the author has presented is refuted by what has already passed about following the conjecture generated from a definitive text, because it is due to its being the ruling of God conjecturally. And this exists in a conjectural text. So requiring action upon one of the two conjectures without the other is arbitrary. Then there remains here a discussion: Grant that the verse indicates the obligation of warning that creates caution upon the warner. But here there is a matter: Knowledge of this obligation and knowledge of it (the obligation'?) For the one who requires definitiveness in usul rules that there must be knowledge of the usul issue. So this is not necessarily required by consensus, for consensus only indicates the obligation to act (upon the warning), not knowledge of it. Until
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the authority of khabar wahid, derived from Quranic verse 9:122. The author analyzes the verse, which commands a group (ta'ifa) to go out and gain understanding in religion to warn their people, so that they may beware (yahdharun). The author argues that the phrase 'that they may beware' implies obligation to act upon the warning, and 'ta'ifa' includes a single person according to Ibn 'Abbas. If acting upon a single person's report were not obligatory, the warning would be pointless. An objection is considered: perhaps the warning is intended to produce certainty through multiple reports reaching tawatur. The response is that the verse indicates immediate caution, not waiting for tawatur. Then the passage addresses the attempt to restrict the verse to fatwa for the common people, excluding riwaya for mujtahids. This is refuted as arbitrary; the apparent meaning includes both. The author further discusses the epistemological status: the verse's indication is considered 'khass' (specific) and thus definitive, not conjectural. A second refutation via consensus on following conjecture is examined but deemed weak because it would make all furu' issues definitive. The author then presents a novel verification by Muttali' al-Asrar: combining the verse with consensus can produce certainty about the obligation to act upon khabar wahid, even if the verse itself only creates conjecture. However, this does not make the furu' issues themselves certain, only the obligation to act. The passage concludes with a reminder that the verse proves the obligation of warning, but for usul, one needs certainty about the obligation itself, which is distinct from acting upon it.

Key Terms
الإنذار (al-indhār): Warning; conveying information to make people cautious. In this context, it refers to reporting rulings to bring about heedfulness.
الظن (al-ẓann): Conjecture or probable knowledge, which is sufficient for action in legal matters according to the majority, but not for usul al-fiqh according to some.
الخاص (al-khāṣṣ): A specific term that is definitive in meaning and does not admit generality. Here, 'ta'ifa' is considered khass, not 'amm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the objection that 'indhar' in Surah 9:122 means only fatwa, not narration of hadith?
A) The verse is abrogated by another verse
B) Restricting to fatwa is arbitrary; the apparent meaning includes both
C) The verse only applies to the Prophet's time
D) Fatwa and narration are the same thing

Key Takeaway
Surah al-Tawbah 9:122, through the classification of 'ta'ifa' as khass and the implication of 'yahdharun', provides a definitive proof in Hanafi usul that the report of a single narrator obligates action.

Al-Quran as Source
advanced
15 min
Deriving the Authority of Khabar al-Wahid from Surah al-Tawbah 9:122
Analyzing the verse of nafr as evidence for accepting single-narrator reports

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the Hanafi argument from the verse of nafr for the authority of khabar al-wahid and evaluate the objections against it.

Core Idea
The verse of nafr proves khabar al-wahid is binding; relying on consensus transforms its conjectural implication into definitive obligation.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut examines a foundational usul argument: does Surah al-Tawbah 9:122 obligate us to accept single-narrator reports? The Hanafi school says yes, but not simply based on the verse alone. As you will see, the argument requires a multi-layered response to objections and a strategic combination with consensus. Understanding this reasoning is critical for mastering how Hanafi usul validates khabar al-wahid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verse and initial inference
The verse commands each group to warn others. The Hanafi argues: if the warning were not to be acted upon, the command would be pointless. This establishes a prima facie obligation.
2
Examine the first objection and response
The objection that the warning aims to achieve tawattur is rejected because the verse links warning directly to achieving caution, so waiting for mass transmission is contrary to the apparent meaning.
The verse says 'لعلهم يحذرون' (so that they may be cautious), which implies immediate effect.
3
Analyze the main dispute over the meaning of 'warning'
The objector restricts 'warning' to fatwa for laymen. The Hanafi response is that this restriction is arbitrary (tahakkum); the term is absolute, covering both fatwa and hadith narration. Furthermore, 'ta'ifah' is absolute, which in Hanafi usul is definitive (qat'i).
In Hanafi usul, the absolute (mutlaq) is considered definitive as a type of specific (khass).
4
Understand the second defense via consensus
Even if the verse only provides conjecture, consensus proves that acting on such conjecture (when arising from a definitive text) is obligatory. This makes the obligation of acting on khabar al-wahid definitive. However, this defense is nuanced and requires careful epistemological classification.
The passage notes that this cumulative evidence approach may not satisfy those who require certainty in usul principles, but the Hanafi view maintains it suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يحصل الحذر فلا ينتظر إلى عدد التواتر واستبعد) هذا الاستدلال بأن (المراد بالإنذار (الفتوى) للعامة، لا رواية الحديث للمجتهد، وهو في غير النزاع ولو سلم أن المراد الرواية (فظاهر) أي فقوله تعالى المذكور ظاهر (وهو) بظنية (لا يكفي (ههنا لكون المسألة أصولية ( ويدفع الاستبعاد ( بأن التخصيص) بالفتوى (تحكم) بل الظاهر الإنذار مطلقاً للعامة بالفتوى وللخاصة برواية الأحاديث والعام قاطع فلا ظنية، بل ليس ههنا عموم فإن الطائفة مطلق وهو من الخاص، والخاص مقطوع اتفاقاً، وهذا إنما يتم لو أنهم اكتفوا بالقطع بالمعنى الأعم في الأصول كما هو الظاهر فافهم واستقم (وقد يدفع) الاستبعاد ثانياً (بالإجماع على وجوب اتباع للظن، وهو واجب العمل بالإجماع فيلغو التمسك بالكريمة (وهو) أي هذا الدفع (ضعيف) لأن من لم يكتف بالظن في الأصول لم يكتف بالدليل الإجمالي فيها أيضاً (لجريانه في الفروع) بأن يقال : وجوب الوتر ،مظنون والظن واجب الاتباع فتصير الفروع كلها قطعيات، فلا يكفي هذا الدليل الإجمالي ههنا بل لا بد من دليل قاطع في كل مسألة مسألة، ولا يظهر لابتناء الكلام على عدم اعتبار الظن ،وجه اللهم إلا بأن يقال : الدليل الإجمالي لا يفيد القطع وإلاّ أفاد في الفروع المظنونية، أيضاً، فلا يكتفي به من يشترط القطع في الأصول، كما أقول: على أن الخصم يمنع الإجماع على اتباع الظن مطلقاً، بل على ما هو قطعي المتن) ظني الدلالة والظن الحادث من قطعي المتن واجب الاتباع بالإجماع واتباع الظن الحادث من ظني المتن متنازع فيه (فافهم) ولمطلع الأسرار الإلهية قدس سره تحقيق بديع هو الدليل الإجمالي عند الانضمام إلى التفصيلي إن أفاد القطع يجب اعتباره كيف لا وإلا يلزم هدر العلم الموجود، وههنا يفيد لأن مقتضى الكريمة وجوب العمل بالخبر المظنون ظناً والإجماع إذ قد أوجب العمل بهذا الظن الحاصل من قطعي المتن قطعاً لزم وجوب العمل بالخبر قطعاً، وأما الفروع فإن أثبت بهذا الدليل القطع بوجوب العمل، فلا شناعة في الالتزام، إذ العمل بها واجب قطعاً، وإن أريد القطعية بنفس الفروع فلا يفيده هذا الإجماع، وأما ههنا فالمطلوب هو وجوب العمل، وهو لازم وبهذا اندفع الشبهتان على أن ما أورد المصنف يدفع بمثل ما مر من اتباع الظن الحادث من قطعي المتن، إنما هو لكونه حكم الله تعالى ظناً، وهذا حاصل في ظنية المتن، فإيجاب العمل بأحد الظنين دون الآخر تحكم، ثم بقي هنا كلام هو أنه : هب أن الكريمة دلت على وجوب الإنذار الموجب على المنذر ، لكن ههنا أمر أن العلم بهذا الوجوب والعلم به، فمن شرط القطع في الأصول يحكم بأنه لا بد من العلم بالمسألة الأصولية، فهذا لا يلزم من الإجماع فإن الإجماع إنما دل على وجوب العمل بوجوب الإنذار لا العلم به حتى يلزم العلم بوجوب العمل بخبر الواحد، لأن الإجماع على وجوب العمل بالظن الحاصل بظاهر الكتاب لا غير فافهم، فإنه دقيق كأنه يعرف وينكر
Obligatory, and if taking [the warning] were not obligatory, the warning would be devoid of benefit. It could be said: perhaps the warning from each group is to achieve knowledge of the warning after warning until reaching the level of tawattur. It was answered that this is contrary to the apparent [meaning], for the noble verse indicates warning by which caution is achieved, so one does not wait for the number of tawattur. This inference is considered improbable because the intended meaning of 'warning' is fatwa for the common people, not narration of hadith for the mujtahid. And this is not in dispute. Even if it is granted that the intended meaning is narration, then it is apparent, meaning the mentioned verse is apparent, and it is conjectural, insufficient here because the issue is usuli. The improbability is repelled by saying that specifying [the verse] to fatwa is arbitrary. Rather, the apparent meaning is warning absolutely: for the common people by fatwa and for the specialists by narrating hadith. And the general is definitive, so there is no conjecturality; rather, there is no generality here because 'ta'ifah' is absolute and is from the specific, and the specific is definitively agreed upon. This is only complete if they were content with certainty in the more general sense in usul, as is apparent. Understand and be upright. The improbability is repelled secondly by consensus on the obligation of following conjecture, meaning that the noble verse generated a conjecture of the obligation of acting on the basis of a single-narrator report, and the definitive consensus indicated the obligation of acting on the conjecture arising from the Shari'ah. Thus acting on the report becomes obligatory definitively. So it is not objected that then it suffices to say: the report is beneficial for conjecture, and acting on conjecture is obligatory by consensus, so clinging to the verse becomes superfluous. This repulsion is weak because whoever is not content with conjecture in usul is not content with cumulative evidence in it either, due to its application in furu'. For example, it could be said: the obligation of witr is conjectural, and conjecture must be followed, so all furu' become definitive. Thus this cumulative evidence is insufficient here; rather, a definitive evidence is needed for each issue. And there is no apparent basis for this, except by saying: cumulative evidence does not bring certainty, otherwise it would bring conjecture in furu' as well. So it is not accepted by those who require certainty in usul. As I say: the opponent denies consensus on following conjecture absolutely, but rather on what is definitive in text with conjectural indication. The conjecture arising from a definitive text is obligatory to follow by consensus, while following conjecture arising from a conjectural text is disputed. Understand. By the one who knows divine secrets (may his secrets be sanctified) there is a novel investigation: cumulative evidence when joined with detailed evidence, if it brings certainty, it must be considered. How could it not? Otherwise, the existing knowledge would be wasted. Here it is beneficial because the implication of the verse is the obligation of acting on a conjectural report conjecturally, and consensus has made acting on this conjecture arising from a definitive text obligatory, so acting on the report becomes obligatory definitively. As for furu', if with this evidence the certainty of the obligation of acting is established, there is no disgrace in committing to it, for acting on them is definitively obligatory. If the certainty of the furu' themselves is intended, this consensus does not benefit it. Here the sought is the obligation of acting, and it is necessary, and by this both doubts are repelled. Moreover, what the author presented is repelled by similar to what passed regarding following conjecture arising from a definitive text, that it is because it is the ruling of Allah conjecturally, and this is present in a conjectural text. So making acting on one of two conjectures obligatory without the other is arbitrary. Then there remains here a statement: suppose the noble verse indicated the obligation of warning which obliges the warner, but here the issue is knowledge of this obligation. So whoever stipulates certainty in usul rules that there must be knowledge of the usuli issue. This is not necessitated by consensus, for consensus only indicated the obligation of acting on the obligation of warning, not knowledge of it, so that knowledge of the obligation of acting on a single-narrator report would be necessitated, because consensus on the obligation of acting on the conjecture obtained from the apparent of the Book only. Understand, for it is subtle, as if it is known and denied.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul argument for the authority of khabar al-wahid (single-narrator report) based on Surah al-Tawbah 9:122 (the verse of nafr). The argument proceeds in layers: first, the verse indicates that warning is obligatory, and if acting on that warning were not obligatory, the warning would be pointless. An objection is raised that perhaps the warning from each group is intended to reach tawattur (mass transmission). The response is that the verse explicitly ties warning to achieving caution, so tawattur is not required. This initial reasoning is then criticized: the objector says the verse actually refers to fatwa (giving legal opinions to laymen), not riwaya (narration for mujtahids). The Hanafi responds that restricting the verse to fatwa is arbitrary; the verse's language is general and covers both fatwa and riwaya. Furthermore, the term 'ta'ifah' is absolute, not general, and the absolute is considered definitive in Hanafi usul, so there is no conjecturality. Then a second defense is offered: even if the verse provides only conjecture, consensus (ijma) proves that acting on such conjecture is obligatory, thus making the obligation definitive. However, this defense is weak because strict usul scholars require certainty in usul principles; cumulative evidence (dalil ijmail) cannot raise conjecture to certainty for them, as it would also make all furu' issues definitive. The passage then presents an insight from a commentator: cumulative evidence combined with detailed evidence can yield certainty if the detailed evidence is from a definitive text. Here, the verse's implication is conjectural, but consensus on acting on conjecture from a definitive text (the Qur'an) transforms the obligation into a definitive one. The discussion ends by clarifying that the real requirement is knowledge of the ruling, not just obligation; consensus only establishes obligation, not knowledge, for those who demand certainty in usul. The author concludes that this is a subtle point. The passage is a classic Hanafi effort to justify khabar al-wahid using both primary text and secondary consensus.

Key Terms
الإنذار (al-inzar): Warning; in this context, it includes both giving fatwa to laymen and narrating hadith to scholars.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report transmitted by one or a few narrators, not reaching tawattur.
مطلق (mutlaq): Absolute term; one that is unrestricted by a qualifying condition.
تحكم (tahakkum): Arbitrary ruling; an unjustified restriction of a term's meaning.
الإجماع (ijma): Consensus of qualified scholars on a legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary objection to using Surah al-Tawbah 9:122 as proof for khabar al-wahid according to the passage?
A) The verse is abrogated by a later revelation.
B) The verse refers specifically to fatwa, not hadith narration.
C) The verse requires tawattur for the warning.
D) The verse only applies to the companions, not later generations.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates khabar al-wahid by combining the verse of nafr (which gives a definitive absolute meaning or, at minimum, conjecture) with consensus that acting on such conjecture from a definitive text is obligatory, thus establishing the obligation definitively.

Al-Quran as Source

Condition of Islam at Performance and Ruling on Innovators' Narrations
Condition of Islam at performance and dispute over innovators' narrations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that Islam is required at the time of narration (ada') and explain the rationale, and evaluate the dispute over accepting narrations from innovators citing the key proofs and the author's position.

Core Idea
Islam is required at narration, not hearing; innovators' narrations are absolutely rejected by some Hanafis.

We continue exploring the conditions for an acceptable narrator in hadith. Previously we covered conditions like being Muslim at the time of hearing. Now we address the condition of being Muslim at the time of narrating, and then a prolonged discussion about narrations from innovators—those who follow heretical beliefs. The author, 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Bukhari, presents a strong position that opposes many earlier Hanafis, arguing that any innovator's narration is unacceptable in religious matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two temporal conditions for narrator
First, note that Islam is required at the time of performance (ada'), not at hearing (tahammul). The example of Jubeir proves this: he heard the Quran as a pagan captive but narrated as a Muslim. The rationale is that hearing only needs discernment (tamyiz), which an unbeliever can have, but narrating requires trustworthiness (adala), which an unbeliever lacks.
This is the rule stated at the beginning: 'Islam at the time of performance, not at hearing'.
2
Understand the Quranic evidence for the condition at performance
The verse 'If a fasiq comes to you with news, verify' (49:6) is cited. In the earlier custom, 'fasiq' included both unbeliever and sinner. Thus, to avoid verification in every case, the narrator must not be a fasiq at the time of narration. This establishes the condition of Islam and uprightness at performance.
The dalala is zanni, but the thubut is qati (Quran).
3
Trace the extension from 'fasiq' to innovators
The author argues that innovators (ahl al-bida) are analogous to fasiq because their false beliefs can lead to lying in hadith. He quotes Fakhr al-Islam distinguishing between testimony (which some Hanafis accept from innovators) and hadith (which they do not). Then the author himself disagrees and says all innovators' narrations are rejected, even if not callers, because their false belief necessarily makes them callers.
The author's position is stricter than many Hanafis.
4
Analyze the madhhab dispute and the author's tarjih
The pre-populated madhhab_dispute_map summarizes the views: Most Hanafis and some Shafi'is (al-Amidi) and Malik accept narrations from innovators who are not callers; the author rejects absolutely. The author cites Ibn Sirin's warning to support his position. He refutes the practice of Bukhari and Muslim by noting the issue is disputed.
The author's tarjih is absolute rejection, aligning with caution.
5
Distinguish between innovators who permit lying and those who do not
The author clarifies at the end: the dispute only applies to innovators who do not permit lying (like Khawarij, Mu'tazila). Those who permit lying (e.g., Karramiyya, extremist Rafidites) are never accepted.
This is an important qualification.

And among them is Islam at the time of performance, not at the time of hearing. And the lack of condition at the time of hearing is the acceptance of Jubeir reciting Surah al-Tur in Maghrib, even though he heard it when he came as a captive on the day of Badr, by consensus. And because discrimination suffices for hearing, which relies on intellect, and the intellect of an unbeliever is not sound. As for conditioning at performance, because He said: 'If a fasiq comes to you with news, verify' (49:6). And 'fasiq' in the earlier custom includes the unbeliever and the sinner in our custom, which is the believer committing a major sin. And the innovation that entails disbelief, like anthropomorphism, is like disbelief according to the group that declares such innovation disbelief, like the two Qadis (Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Abdul Jabbar of the Mu'tazila). And according to others, there is a distinction between essential disbelief and commitment; the one who commits is an unbeliever, unlike the one to whom it applies but he does not see it nor believe it. And like the clear innovations (bida' jaliyya), which are innovations not based on a strong legally acceptable ambiguity, such that it is no excuse legally, neither in this world nor the hereafter, like the sin of the Khawarij who permit the blood and wealth of Muslims and captivity of their children. Regarding the clear innovation, acceptance of narration is upon the view of most non-investigating Hanafis, and it is the chosen view of those after them, contrary to al-Amidi (from the Shafi'is) and those who follow him, and Imam Malik, and most Hanafis, and it is the chosen view of this servant. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: As for the follower of desire (sahib al-hawa), our scholars have acted upon their testimonies except the Khattabiyya, because the follower of desire fell into it due to his deep engagement, and that prevents him from lying, so it is not a valid suspicion except for one who is religiously committed to believing the claimant if he affiliates with his sect, he is suspected of falsehood and forgery, like the Khattabiyya. Similarly, one who says that inspiration is a proof, his testimony must not be accepted. As for the sunan, it is said that the chosen madhhab among us is that the narration of one who has adopted desire and innovation and calls people to it is not accepted, and upon this are all the imams of fiqh and hadith. Because arguing and calling to desire is a cause inciting false speech, so he is not trusted for his hadith. But testimony in the rights of people is not like that, because that does not call to forgery in that field, so his testimony is not rejected. So this means that the follower of desire is like the sinner in the field of sunan and hadith. End of his noble words. And in my view: His statement 'who has adopted' is of the type of using the manifest in place of the implied. The conclusion is that the people of innovation, their narrations are not accepted, as is indicated by his statement at the end. He only used the manifest to indicate that all the people of desire are adopters of innovation and callers to it, so their narrations are not accepted in any religious matter at all, as narrated from Muhammad ibn Sirin: 'The sunan are your religion, so see from whom you take your religion.' The author of Al-Kashf interpreted his statement to mean that if the innovator calls people to his innovation, he is not accepted; otherwise he is accepted. What compelled him to this interpretation is the existence in the sound collections of narrations from innovators. For Muhammad ibn Ismail al-Bukhari narrated in his Sahih from 'Ubbad ibn Ya'qub, and Imam Abu Bakr ibn Ishaq ibn Khuzayma said: 'He narrated to us the truthful in his narration, suspect in his religion, 'Ubbad ibn Ya'qub.' And Bukhari argued with Muhammad ibn Ziyad and Hariz ibn 'Uthman, both known for nasb, and Bukhari and Muslim agreed on arguing with Abu Mu'awiya Muhammad ibn Hazim ibn Musa, both known for exaggeration. And in this is clear consideration. For when the follower of desire claims in his false belief that it is correct and that it is the Muhammadan shari'a, and that commanding the good is obligatory according to him, then he must be a caller to his desire. So to assume he is not a caller to his desire is either impossible or contradicts justice because he commits a religious offense in his view. Also, it contradicts his statement at the end of the research. If the desire is confirmed to be like the sinner, and the extraction of Bukhari, Muslim, and Muhammad ibn Ishaq is not proof, because the issue is disputed, so the claim of one group is not proof against the other. How, while like Imam Muhammad ibn Sirin, the imam of imams of hadith, refrained from narrating from them. And more surprising than this interpretation is what some have interpreted: that what he said is only about non-clear innovation, but in clear innovation, their narrations are accepted even if they are callers. How can that be? It is not supported by his initial statement and has no trace or effect. Since calling to non-clear innovation necessitates suspicion and non-acceptance, then clear innovation is even more so. So it has become clear to you that the narration of the people of innovation is absolutely not accepted according to this great scholar. Then know that the dispute is concerning the innovators who do not permit lying. As for those who permit it, like the Karramiyya, their narration is never accepted, because when lying is permissible in their religion in their belief, they do not care to commit it. Among them are the extremist Rafidites. And 'Ubaydullah... this was a companion.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage discusses two conditions for narrators: (1) Islam is required at the time of narrating (ada'), not at the time of hearing (tahammul), as exemplified by Jubeir, who heard as a pagan captive but narrated as a Muslim. (2) The narrator must be upright ('adala), which excludes fasiq (sinner) and, by extension, innovators (ahl al-bida'). The author then delves into the dispute over accepting narrations from innovators. Among Hanafis, there is disagreement: some accept narrations from innovators who are not calling to innovation (e.g., Fakhr al-Islam accepts their testimony but not their hadith), while the author (likely 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Bukhari) argues for absolute rejection of innovator's narrations in religious matters, citing Ibn Sirin's famous warning. The author refutes the practice of Bukhari and Muslim who narrated from innovators by noting that the issue is disputed and thus not a proof. He distinguishes between innovators who permit lying (e.g., Karramiyya) and those who do not; the former are never accepted. The passage ends abruptly discussing 'Ubaydullah.

Key Terms
فاسق (fasiq): In the earlier custom, includes unbelievers and sinners; in later custom, it is a believer who commits a major sin.
بدعة جلية (bid'a jaliyya): A clear innovation not based on a strong legal ambiguity, such as the beliefs of the Khawarij.
صاحب هوى (sahib hawa): One who follows his desires in religious matters, i.e., an innovator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why must all innovators be considered callers to their innovation?
A) Because they actively preach in public.
B) Because they believe their innovation is true and consider it obligatory to command good.
C) Because they are all known to lie.
D) Because the Quran commands it.

Key Takeaway
The author of this text rejects all narrations from innovators absolutely, arguing that every innovator is necessarily a caller to innovation, and even non-callers are still suspect.

Evidence for and Against Accepting Narrations from Ahl al-Bid'ah
Analyses proofs from hadith, Sahaba practice, and Quran regarding ahl al-bid'ah narrations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main proofs cited for accepting narrations from ahl al-bid'ah, evaluate their weaknesses using Hanafi usul principles, and articulate the operative Hanafi position.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school rejects narrations from clear innovators because the Quran commands investigation, and counterevidence is weak or misapplied.

This passage takes you through a classic usul debate: can we accept hadith reports from narrators known for clear innovation (ahl al-bid'ah al-jaliyyah)? The author presents three arguments from those who accept them—a hadith, the practice of the Sahaba with the killers of Uthman, and a reading of Quran 49:6—and systematically refutes each. As you work through the proofs, pay careful attention to how the author critiques authenticity, scope, and logical force. By the end, you will see why the Hanafi position relies on the Quran's explicit command to verify news from any fasiq.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the hadith proof
Identify the hadith 'I am commanded to judge by the apparent' and the claim that it supports accepting narrations based on outward righteousness. Then examine the Hanafi rebuttal: the hadith is weak (da'if) and, even if sound, applies only to judicial rulings, not to accepting religious reports.
The hadith is declared without basis by al-Dhahabi.
2
Examine the Sahaba practice proof
Consider the argument that the Sahaba accepted testimony and narrations from the killers of Uthman (Khawarij). Then trace the Hanafi response: there was no consensus—direct killers were not accepted, and others were only accepted after repentance; senior Sahaba are not recorded accepting them.
The counter-argument that the killers were not clearly innovators is dismissed because killing Uthman was a grave sin and considering it lawful is disbelief.
3
Evaluate the Quranic proof
Look at the verse 'If a sinful person comes to you with news, investigate' (49:6). The Hanafi position is that innovators are fasiq (they transgress legal limits), so their reports require investigation, not blind acceptance. Understand that the verse is general and applies to all fasiq, including innovators.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut and qat'i al-dalala in commanding investigation.
4
Synthesize the Hanafi position
Combine the three rebuttals to see the consistent Hanafi ruling: narrations from ahl al-bid'ah al-jaliyyah are not accepted unless the narrator repents. Note the nuance that the innovator's blood remains inviolable; they are not declared disbelievers.
This position protects the Sunnah from unreliable reports while maintaining social cohesion.

Salamah ibn... The second principle/the Sunnah on the summary of what preceded, i.e., acceptance of people of clear innovation due to what passed and due to his statement: 'I have been commanded to judge by the apparent, and the apparent state of them is truthfulness.' We say: The hadith is not authentic in al-Taysir. Al-Dhahabi and others said it has no basis, and it is transmitted from some hadith scholars that it is weak. And what is in the Two Sahihs from al-Akwa' that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'I am only a human like you. You bring disputes to me, and perhaps one of you is more eloquent in argument, so I judge for him according to what I hear. So if I judge for him something from the right of his brother, let him not take it, for I only cut off a piece of fire for him.' This only indicates judgment according to the apparent argument, not acceptance of narration according to the apparent. If you say: It indicates by the concept of harmony, I say: No, for the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) only accepted testimony according to the apparent, and no one understands from it religion from one whose apparent is righteousness and inner is corruption at all. (And what is in al-Mukhtasar that it is abandoned in the apparent with the disbeliever and the sinful person whose truthfulness is assumed) So the hadith is specific to those other than them. (It is rebutted) by the fact that the assumption of truthfulness of the sinful person or disbeliever is not real, because certainty of sin contradicts the appearance of truthfulness, for sin is a sign of lying, so ponder. The rebuttal is insufficient, for sin does not necessarily cause lying definitively. How many sinful people avoid lying more than others do due to their manliness and status, and likewise some disbelievers. And objection may be raised against al-Mukhtasar that specification of the hadith does not invalidate its authority in the remaining. The answer: The noble verse is carried on the apparent, and the hadith is abandoned, so it does not oppose it. Then nothing is refuted, but there remains that the verse is also specific to matters other than transactions. So it is better to say that the hadith is specific to other than the sinful and the people of clear innovation from the sinful. And what sin is greater than their sin? So ponder. (And they argued for acceptance of people of innovation) that the Companions used to accept the killers of Amir al-Mu'minin Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) in testimony and narration, and they were among the worst of the Khawarij. (They were answered by denying consensus on acceptance) for the direct perpetrators were not accepted at all. As for the non-direct perpetrators involved in the tribulation, some were accepted after repentance and others were not, except what came from some governors, and they are not considered. And it is not transmitted from the senior Companions at all, let alone consensus. (And) they were answered by denying consensus on clarity, i.e., clarity of innovation. So this is not from clear innovation, but rather it is considered jurisprudential disbelief. And this answer is nothing, for Amir al-Mu'minin and the Imam of the two parties Uthman ibn Affan is a true Imam with lofty merits. The Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings upon him and his family and companions) testified that he is from the people of Paradise, and by his testimony and that he has great virtue among the religious. So his killing is a major sin, and considering it permissible is disbelief. So it is not jurisprudential at all, and there is no room for ijtihad in it, and no place for doubt. If there is, it is not strong nor considered legally. So the innovation is clearly clear, so understand and be certain. The investigators who do not accept people of innovation said: Allah the Exalted said: 'If a sinful person comes to you with news, investigate' [al-Hujurat:6]. And what sin is more heinous than bad beliefs? I say to you: Do you deny that the religious person from the people of the qiblah claiming to follow the Muhammadan religion is a sinful person according to the previous custom upon which the noble Quran was revealed? This denial is nothing, for the sinful person is the one who goes beyond the legal limit, and upon that the Quran was revealed. There is no doubt that the innovator is transgressing the limit, taking a path other than the straight legal path, so he is definitely a sinful person. Yes: his blood is inviolable absolutely, no doubt in it for anyone.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on accepting narrations from ahl al-bid'ah. It presents three main proofs advanced by those who accept them, and the Hanafi refutations. First, the hadith 'I am commanded to judge by the apparent' — the author refutes it by noting that the hadith is weak (da'if) and, even if accepted, only applies to judging between litigants based on evidence, not to accepting narrations about religion. The concept of 'mukhalafah' (opposite implication) is rejected because the Prophet only accepted testimony by apparent, not narrations. Second, the claim that the Sahaba accepted reports from the killers of Uthman (Khawarij) — the author refutes by denying consensus: direct killers were not accepted, some non-direct ones were accepted only after repentance, and senior Sahaba are not reported to have accepted them. The counterargument that the killers were not clearly innovators is dismissed because killing Uthman was a grave sin and considering it lawful is disbelief, so the innovation is clear. Third, the Quranic verse 'If a sinful person comes to you with news, investigate' — the author argues that innovators are definitely sinful because they transgress the legal limit, thus their reports require verification. The passage concludes by affirming that ahl al-bid'ah are fasiqun, though their blood remains inviolable (they are not disbelievers).

Key Terms
أهل البدع الجلية (Ahl al-bid'ah al-jaliyyah): People of clear innovation, such as those who consider the killing of Uthman lawful or hold other extreme beliefs that clearly contradict established Islamic teachings.
فسق (Fisq): Legal sinfulness, going beyond the legal limit; innovators are considered fasiq because they deviate from the straight path.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul analysis in this passage, what is the status of the hadith 'I am commanded to judge by the apparent'?
A) Sahih and directly relevant to narration acceptance.
B) Weak (da'if) and only applies to judicial rulings, not narration acceptance.
C) Strong (hasan) but abrogated by Quran 49:6.
D) Applies only to testimony in court, not to hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafis reject narrations from clear innovators because they are fasiq and the Quran mandates investigation; the counterarguments from hadith and Sahaba practice are insufficient due to weakness and lack of consensus.

Accepting Narrations from Clear Innovators: A Usul Dispute
Dispute about accepting hadith from clear innovators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that a clear innovator (sahib bid'a jalīya) is considered fasiq and therefore their narrations are not accepted, and you will be able to evaluate the opposing evidence from hadith and practice.

Core Idea
Clear innovators are fasiq; the Quran commands rejecting their unsupported narrations.

This passage dives into a key dispute in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: should we accept hadith narrations from someone who holds a clear innovation (bid'a jalīya)? The Hanafi position, supported by the verifiers (muhaqqiqūn), is a firm no: they are fasiq and their reports are unreliable. But what about the hadith 'I judge by the apparent' and the Companions' acceptance from those involved in the killing of 'Uthman? In this lesson, you'll dissect the arguments and understand why the Hanafi school prioritizes the Quranic command to investigate when a fasiq brings news.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
What is the specific issue? Is it about accepting narrations from clear innovators?
The dispute is over the acceptance of narrations from ahl al-bid'a al-jaliya.
2
State the Hanafi position and its primary evidence
The Hanafis reject because the narrator is fasiq; cite Quran 49:6.
The verse commands investigation, implying non-acceptance without verification.
3
Examine the opposing evidence
What proof does the other side bring? (hadith 'I judge by the apparent' and Companions' practice with Qatla 'Uthman).
The hadith suggests apparent righteousness suffices; the practice suggests acceptance.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school refute the opposing evidence? (hadith weak, companion practice conditional).
Al-Dhahabi declares the hadith baseless; the acceptance was limited and not from senior Companions.
5
Determine the outcome
Which position is preferred and why? (Quranic evidence is qat'i, hadith is da'if).
The Hanafi position is stronger in usul due to the weight of the proof.

يقبلون قتلة أمير المؤمنين (عثمان) رضي الله تعالى عنه (شهادة ورواية) وهم من أخبث الخوارج (وأجيب بمنع الإجماع على القبول) فإن المباشرين للقتل لم يقبل قولهم أصلاً، وأما غير المباشرين الداخلين في البلوى
